The Maxim: When Literal Meaning is Impossible, Resort to Metaphor
Explains a qawida with furuat in talaq and itq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'When the literal meaning is impossible, resort to the metaphorical meaning' to cases of ambiguous talaq and manumission, and identify the differences between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
If a husband says 'one of you is divorced' to his wife and a wall, the divorce falls on the wife—because the literal meaning is impossible, so the word is taken metaphorically to avoid nullification.

The Hanafi school employs a powerful usul principle: when the literal application of a word is impossible, one must resort to a metaphorical meaning that the word can bear, rather than render the speech null. This maxim is crucial in cases where a husband addresses a wife and another entity with the word 'divorce'. The passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents the core ruling and its branches, illustrating how this maxim resolves ambiguity in talaq and itq, and shows an intra-Hanafi difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base case and the ruling
Read the opening part: if a husband says 'one of you is divorced' to his wife and another woman, without intention. State the difference: according to Abu Hanifa the wife is not divorced, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say she is divorced.
Focus on the ambiguity of the address.
2
Observe the exception
When the other entity is inanimate (wall, animal) or not a proper subject of divorce, the divorce falls on the wife according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (Muhammad dissents).
Why does this exception exist?
3
Connect to the maxim
The literal meaning cannot refer to a wall or animal for divorce, so the word is applied metaphorically to the wife. This is the maxim 'when literal is impossible, resort to metaphor'.
The maxim avoids nullifying the speech.
4
Extend to other furuat
Apply the same principle to a case where a man says to his older slave 'this is my son' – Abu Hanifa interprets it as metaphor for freeing the slave. Contrast with the Sahibayn who reject metaphor here.
Also note the consensus when saying 'one of you is free' to a slave and an animal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طلقت امرأته. ولو قال إحداكما طالق، ولم ينو شيئًا لا تطلق امرأته. وعن أبي يوسف ومحمد أنها تطلق ولو جمع بين امرأته وبين ما ليس محلاً للطلاق كالبهيمة والحجر، وقال إحداكما طالق طلقت امرأته في قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد لا تطلق ولو جمع بين امرأته الحيَّة والميتة وقال إحداكما طالق لا تطلق الحيَّة. ثم قال فيها ولو جمع بين امرأتين إحداهما صحيحة النكاح والأخرى فاسدة النكاح وقال إحداكما طالق لا تطلق صحيحة النكاح، كما لو جمع بين منكوحته وأجنبية وقال إحداكما طالق. (انتهى). وحاصله أنه لو جمع بين امرأته وغيرها وقال إحداكما طالق لم يقع على امرأته في جميع الصور، إلا إذا جمع بينهما وبين جدار أو بهيمة لأن الجدار لما لم يكن أهلاً للطلاق أعمل اللفظ في امرأته بخلاف ما إذا كان المفهوم آدميا فإنه صالح في الجملة، إلا أنه يشكل بالرجل فإنه لا يوصف بالطلاق عليه، ولذا لو قال لها أنا منك طالق لغي. وقد يقال إن الطلاق لإزالة الوصلة وهي مشتركة بينهما. ومما فرعته على القاعدة قول الإمام الأعظم إذا قال لعبده الأكبر سنًا منه: هذا ابني. فإنه أعمله عتقا مجازا عن هذا حرّ، وهما أهملاه وقال في المنار من بحث الحروف من أو وقالا إذا قال لعبده ودابته هذا حرّ أو هذا إنه باطل لأنه اسم لأحدهما غير معين وذلك غير محل للعتق وعنده هو كذلك لكن على احتمال التعيين حتى لزمه التعيين كما في مسألة العبدين والعمل بالمحتمل أولى من الإهدار فجعل ما وضع لحقيقته مجازا عما يحتمله وإن استحالت حقيقته، وهما ينكران الاستعارة عند استحالة الحكم. (انتهى). قيّد بأو لأنه لو قال لعبده ودابته أحدكما حرّ عتق بالإجماع كما في المحيط وبيَّنا الفرق في شرح المنار.
His wife is divorced. If he said 'one of you is divorced' and did not intend anything, his wife is not divorced. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, she is divorced. If he combined between his wife and something not subject to divorce, like an animal or stone, and said 'one of you is divorced', his wife is divorced according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said she is not divorced. If he combined between his living wife and a dead wife and said 'one of you is divorced', the living one is not divorced. Then he said therein: If he combined between two wives, one with a valid marriage and the other with an invalid marriage, and said 'one of you is divorced', the valid wife is not divorced, just as if he combined between his married wife and a stranger and said 'one of you is divorced'. (End). The summary is that if he combined between his wife and another and said 'one of you is divorced', it does not fall on his wife in all cases, except if he combined between them and a wall or animal, because the wall is not eligible for divorce, so the word is applied to his wife, unlike if the understood entity is a human, who is generally eligible, except it is problematic with a man, as he is not described as being divorced, so if he said to her 'I am divorced from you', it is nonsense. It may be said that divorce is to remove the connection, which is shared between them. Among the branches I derived from the maxim is the saying of the Great Imam (Abu Hanifa): if he says to his slave who is older than him 'this is my son', he applies it metaphorically as freeing him, and both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disregard it. And it is said in al-Manar from the section on particles 'from' and 'or', and they said: if he says to his slave and his animal 'this is free' or 'this', it is invalid because it is a name for one of them unspecified, and that is not a place for emancipation, and with him (Abu Hanifa) it is also like that but on the probability of specification, so specification becomes necessary as in the case of two slaves, and acting on the probable is better than nullification, so he made what was placed for its literal meaning a metaphor for what it can bear, even if its literal meaning is impossible. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) reject metaphor when the ruling is impossible. (End). It is restricted with 'or' because if he says to his slave and his animal 'one of you is free', he is free by consensus as in al-Muhit, and we explained the difference in Sharh al-Manar.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi rulings on the expression 'one of you is divorced' (إحداكما طالق) when addressed to a wife and another entity. The base ruling: if a husband says this to his wife and another woman, without specification, according to Abu Hanifa the wife is not divorced, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that she is divorced. The discussion then extends to cases where the other is non-human (animal, stone) or a dead wife, etc. The summary principle: the divorce only falls on the wife if the other entity is not an actual addressable person for divorce (like a wall or animal), because then the word 'you' is forced to refer to the wife. The passage also relates this to a broader maxim: when the literal meaning is impossible, give preference to a metaphorical meaning to avoid nullifying the speech entirely. This is applied to a case where a man says to his older slave 'this is my son' – Abu Hanifa interprets this as a metaphor for freeing the slave, while the two disciples reject this because emancipation is not possible by mere declaration without intention. However, in the case of saying 'one of you is free' to a slave and an animal, consensus holds that the slave is freed because the word 'one of you' forces a specific reference. The discussion illustrates the Hanafi methodology of employing metaphorical interpretation (مجاز) to uphold the efficacy of speech when literal application fails.

Key Terms
إحداكما (ihdākumā): Address to two females: 'one of you two'.
طلاق (talāq): Divorce; the legal dissolution of marriage.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage of a word when its literal meaning is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim 'When literal meaning is impossible, resort to metaphor', what is the ruling when a husband says to his wife and a stone: 'one of you is divorced'?
A) The wife is not divorced because of ambiguity.
B) The wife is divorced because the stone is not a proper subject, forcing reference to the wife.
C) The utterance is invalid.
D) Both are considered divorced metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'When the literal is impossible, resort to metaphor' is crucial for preserving the legal effect of speech in ambiguous contexts, especially in talaq and itq.

Metaphorical Extension in Waqf: When Literal Meaning Fails
Applying the maxim of metaphorical interpretation to waqf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'When the literal meaning is impossible, resort to the metaphorical meaning' to resolve ambiguities in waqf conditions.

Core Idea
When literal application fails, metaphor saves the waqf from nullification.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi maxim for interpreting ambiguous waqf conditions. When a donor's words cannot apply literally, the law uses metaphorical meaning to give effect to their intention. We will see how this applies when 'children' in a waqf includes grandchildren, and 'mawālī' includes their clients.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim
Identify the underlying maxim: إذا استحالت الحقيقة يصار إلى المجاز. Understand its rank as a sughra (minor) maxim and its scope in Hanafi law.
This maxim is a dabt (restricting sub-maxim) applied when literal meaning is impossible.
2
Identify the Furuat
Locate the two cases presented: waqf for 'children' when only grandchildren exist, and waqf for 'mawālī' when only mawālī of mawālī exist.
Both cases assume no literal subjects are alive.
3
Apply the Maxim to Each Case
In each case, determine why the literal meaning is impossible (no children, no mawālī). Then, identify the metaphorical meaning that the word can bear (grandchildren for children; mawālī of mawālī for mawālī). Conclude that the waqf benefits those groups.
The maxim requires that the metaphorical meaning be a valid possibility in language and context.
4
Consider Istithna’at
Note that there are no exceptions (istithna’at) to this application. The maxim applies straightforwardly.
If the donor had explicitly excluded grandchildren, the result would differ.
5
Relate to Other Maxims
Understand that this maxim complements 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions) and parallels 'al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk' (certainty is not removed by doubt). Together, they ensure the donor’s intent is honoured.
The related maxims provide a systemic unity in Hanafi legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها لو وقف على أولاده وليس له إلا أولاد أولاد حمل عليهم صونًا للفظ من الإهمال عملاً بالمجاز، وكذا لو وقف على مواليه وليس له موال وإنما له موالي موال استحقوا، كما في التحرير.
And among them: if he makes a waqf for his children but he has no children except grandchildren, they are carried (included) upon them to preserve the word from being disregarded, acting on metaphor. Similarly, if he makes a waqf for his mawālī (clients/freed slaves) but he has no mawālī, only the mawālī of his mawālī, they are entitled, as in al-Tahrir.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This short passage presents another application of the same maxim. When someone makes a waqf (endowment) for his 'children' (أولاد) but literally has no children, only grandchildren (أولاد أولاد), the term 'children' is metaphorically extended to include grandchildren. This is done to give effect to the waqf and avoid making the words meaningless. Similarly, for 'mawālī' (clients), if the person has no direct clients but only clients of his clients, they inherit the benefit. This principle is taken from the work al-Tahrir. The underlying maxim is that when the literal meaning is impossible, the metaphorical meaning is adopted to preserve the efficacy of the utterance.

Key Terms
وقف (waqf): Endowment; dedicating property for charitable or family benefit.
موالي (mawālī): Freed slaves/clients; those connected through wala' (patronage).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person makes a waqf for his 'mawālī' but only has clients of his clients (mawālī of mawālī), what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The waqf is invalid because no direct mawālī exist.
B) The clients of his clients are entitled to the waqf.
C) The waqf goes to the public treasury.
D) The donor must specify again.

Key Takeaway
When literal interpretation is impossible, Hanafi law adopts a feasible metaphorical meaning to preserve the waqf's validity.

Literal Wording in Waqf: Al-Subki's Fatwa on Beneficiary Substitution
qawida with furuat: waqf distribution and tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim of adhering to the literal wording of a waqf founder to resolve disputes over the distribution of shares when beneficiaries die across multiple generations, and construct a fatwa using the same reasoning.

Core Idea
The founder's literal wording governs waqf distribution; inferred intentions are insufficient to override it.

In this lesson, we examine a complex waqf fatwa from Imam al-Subki, which al-Asyuti considered a branch of a maxim regarding literal vs. metaphorical interpretation. You will see how the Hanafi school applies the rule that intentions without textual indication carry no weight, and how this determines the distribution of shares when multiple deaths occur across generations. We will walk through al-Subki's reasoning and the calculation he provides, so you can apply this approach in your own fatwa work.

Guided Path
1
Understand the waqf structure
Read the founder's conditions: the waqf is for himself, then his children, then their children and descendants, with shares for males twice females. Substitution rules: if a beneficiary dies leaving a child or descendant, their share goes to that child; if no descendant, it goes to the closest in rank among the same generation. Also, if a beneficiary dies before ever receiving any benefit, their child takes their place.
Note the distinction between 'dying after receiving' (same as dying after entitlement) and 'dying before entitlement'.
2
Identify the factual scenario
After the initial deaths, the waqf transfers to sons Ahmad and Abd al-Qadir. Then Abd al-Qadir dies, leaving three children (Ali, Amr, Latifa) and two grandchildren from a predeceased son Muhammad (Abd al-Rahman, Malaka). Then further deaths occur: Amr dies childless, Latifa dies leaving a daughter Fatima, Ali dies leaving a daughter Zaynab, and finally Fatima dies childless. The question: to whom does Fatima's share go?
The key dispute is whether the grandchildren of the predeceased son (Abd al-Rahman and Malaka) share in the uncle's (Abd al-Qadir's) share at the first stage.
3
State the apparent Hanafi view and the alternative
Al-Subki says the apparent (zahir) view is that only Abd al-Qadir's direct children (Ali, Amr, Latifa) take his share, in the ratio 2:2:1 (male:female). The alternative view would include Abd al-Rahman and Malaka as substitutes for their father Muhammad, giving them a share alongside the direct children. Under the alternative, the shares would be 2:2:2:1 for Ali, Amr, , , Latifa? Actually the text says 'a possibility that they share, giving them two-sevenths each for Ali, Amr, Abd al-Rahman, and one-seventh for Latifa, and one-seventh for Malaka?' Wait need to check: the translation says 'they would have two-sevenths, Ali two-sevenths, Amr two-sevenths, Latifa one-seventh.' That would be 5 parties: Ali, Amr, Abd al-Rahman, Latifa, Malaka? But the text says 'ولعلي السبعان ولعمرو السبعان وللطيفة السبع' and earlier 'للمحمديين السبعان' meaning for the two children of Muhammad two-sevenths. So I think the alternative distribution is: for the two children of Muhammad (Abd al-Rahman and Malaka together) two-sevenths? Actually it says 'لهما السبعان' i.e., 'to both of them two-sevenths' meaning each gets one-seventh? No, 'السبعان' is dual, meaning two-sevenths total for both? Usually 'سبعان' means two-sevenths, and they are two individuals, so each gets one-seventh. But later it says 'ولعلي السبعان' meaning two-sevenths for Ali and similarly for Amr, and 'وللطيفة السبع' one-seventh for Latifa. So total shares: Ali 2/7, Amr 2/7, Abd al-Rahman 1/7? Actually 'لهما السبعان' could mean both together have two-sevenths, but if each got one-seventh, it would be two individuals each with one-seventh, total 2/7. But then Ali and Amr each have 2/7 total 4/7, plus Latifa 1/7, total 7/7. So Abd al-Rahman and Malaka together get 2/7, each 1/7. So the alternative splits the 2/7 between them equally? Not specified but likely equally. The apparent view is simpler: three children, 2/5, 2/5, 1/5. So the calculations differ.
4
Analyze al-Subki's tarjih (preponderance)
Al-Subki gives three reasons for preponderating the apparent view: (1) The claim that the founder's intention is not to deprive anyone is weak because intentions without wording are not considered. (2) Including the grandchildren would require reinterpreting the order between generations, which is against the apparent. (3) The clause 'whoever dies before entitlement, his child takes his place' only applies if the deceased was a 'person of the waqf' at the time of death – but Muhammad died before his father Abd al-Qadir, so he never actually became a beneficiary, so his children do not qualify as substitutes. This third argument is strong but not sufficient to adopt the alternative because the apparent is more consistent.
Note the use of multiple arguments: textual, intentional, and consistency.
5
Apply the ruling to calculate the final distribution
Based on the apparent view, the share of Abd al-Qadir (let’s assume it's 1/2 of the original waqf? Actually not stated but we work from the final distribution: after all deaths, the entire share of Abd al-Qadir is divided into 60 parts. Al-Subki gives: Abd al-Rahman 22, Malaka 11, Zaynab 27. These numbers come from a careful step-by-step calculation: first, split Abd al-Qadir's share among his three children (2/5, 2/5, 1/5). Then each of those children dies and their shares pass to their own children (or to siblings in case of no children). Follow the lineage and apply the substitution rules strictly. The calculation is given; we can verify that it is consistent with the apparent view.
The exact arithmetic might be complex but the key is the method: follow the founder's conditions literally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس منها ما لو أتى بالشرط والجواب بلا فاء، فإنا لا نقول بالتعليق لعدم إمكانه فيتنجز ولا ينوي خلافًا لما رُوِيَ عن أبي يوسف، وكذا أنت طالق في مكة فيتنجز إلا إذا أراد في دخولك مكة فيدين وإذا دخلت مكة تعليق. وقد جعل الإمام الأسيوطي من فروعها ما وقع في فتاوى السبكي فنذكر كلامهما بالتمام، ثم نذكر ما يسره الله تعالى مما يناسب أصولنا قال السبكي: لو أن رجلاً وقف عليه ثم على أولاده ثم على أولادهم ونسله وعقبه ذكراً وأُنثى، للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين. على أن من توفي منهم عن ولد أو نسل عاد ما كان جاريًا عليه من ذلك على ولده ثم ولد ولده ثم على نسله على الفريضة، وعلى أن من توفي عن غير نسل عاد ما كان جاريًا عليه على من كان في درجته من أهل الوقف المذكور؛ يقدّم الأقرب إليه فالأقرب ويستوي الأخ الشقيق والأخ من الأب. ومن مات من أهل الوقف قبل استحقاقه لشيء من منافع الوقف وترك ولدا أو أسفل منه استحق ما كان استحقه المتوفَّى لو بقي حيا إلى أن يصير إليه شيء من منافع الوقف المذكور، وقام ولده في الاستحقاق مقام المتوفى فإذا انقرضوا فعلى الفقراء، ولو توفي الموقوف عليه وانتقل الوقف إلى ولديه أحمد وعبد القادر، ثم توفي عبد القادر، وترك ثلاثة أولاد وهم: علي وعمرو ولطيفة وولدي ابنه محمد المتوفى حال حياة والده؛ وهما عبد الرحمن وملكة ثم توفي عمرو عن غير نسل ثم توفيت لطيفة وتركت بنتا تسمى فاطمة ثم توفي علي وترك بنتا تسمى زينب ثم توفيت فاطمة بنت لطيفة عن غير نسل. فإلى مَن ينتقل نصيب فاطمة المذكورة؟ فأجاب: الذي ظهر لي الآن أن نصيب عبد القادر جميعه يقسم من هذا الوقف على ستين جزءا لعبد الرحمن منها اثنان وعشرون، ولملكة أحد عشر، ولزينب سبعة وعشرون ولا يستمر هذا الحكم في أعقابهم، بل كل وقت بحسبه. قال: وبيان ذلك أن عبد القادر لما توفي انتقل نصيبه إلى أولاده الثلاثة وهم علي وعمرو ولطيفة، للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، فلعلي خمساه، ولعمرو خمساه، وللطيفة خمسه. وهذا هو الظاهر عندنا ويحتمل أنه يقال يشاركهم عبد الرحمن وملكة ولدا محمد المتوفّى في حياة أبيه ونزلا منزلة أبيهما فيكون لهما السبعان، ولعلي السبعان، ولعمرو السبعان وللطيفة السبع وهذا وإن كان محتملاً، فهو مرجوح عندنا لأن التمكن في مأخذه ثلاثة أمور. أحدها: أن مقصود الواقف أن لا يحرم أحد من ذريته وهذا ضعيف لأن المقاصد إذا لم يدل عليها اللفظ لا يعتبر. الثاني: إدخالهم في الحكم وجعل الترتيب بين كل أصل وفرعه لا بين الطبقتين جميعًا، وهذا محتمل لكنه خلاف الظاهر. وقد كنت ملتُ إليه مرة في وقف للفظ اقتضاه فيه لست أعمه في كل ترتيب. الثالث: الاستناد إلى قول الواقف؛ إن من مات من أهل الوقف قبل استحقاقه لشيء قام ولده مقامه. وهذا قوي لكن إنما يتم لو صدق على المتوفى في حياة والده أنه من أهل الوقف.
And among them is not what if he comes with the condition and response without 'fa', for we do not say it is conditional because it is impossible, so it becomes immediate and he does not intend, contrary to what is narrated from Abu Yusuf. Similarly, 'you are divorced in Mecca' is immediate unless he intended 'when you enter Mecca', then he is believed inwardly, and if she enters Mecca, it is conditional. And Imam al-Asyuti considered among its branches what is found in the fatwas of al-Subki, so we mention both their words completely, then we mention what Allah facilitates that is suitable for our principles. Al-Subki said: If a man made a waqf for himself, then for his children, then for their children and descendants and offspring, male and female, with the male having twice the share of the female. Provided that whoever of them dies leaving a child or descendant, what was running for him returns to his child, then to his child's child, then to his descendants according to the prescribed division. And provided that whoever dies without descendant, what was running for him goes to those in his rank among the people of the aforementioned waqf, giving precedence to the closest then the closer, and the full brother and consanguine brother are equal. And whoever from the people of the waqf dies before becoming entitled to any benefit from the waqf and leaves a child or lower, they are entitled to what the deceased would have been entitled to if he had remained alive until something from the benefits of the mentioned waqf came to him, and his child takes his place in entitlement. Then when they are extinct, it goes to the poor. And if the waqf-endowed died and the waqf transferred to his two sons Ahmad and Abd al-Qadir, then Abd al-Qadir died, leaving three children: Ali, Amr, Latifa, and the two children of his son Muhammad who died during his father's life: Abd al-Rahman and Malaka. Then Amr died without descendant, then Latifa died leaving a daughter named Fatima, then Ali died leaving a daughter named Zaynab, then Fatima bint Latifa died without descendant. So to whom does the share of the mentioned Fatima transfer? He answered: What appears to me now is that the entire share of Abd al-Qadir is divided from this waqf into sixty parts: for Abd al-Rahman twenty-two, for Malaka eleven, for Zaynab twenty-seven, and this ruling does not continue among their descendants, but each time according to its situation. He said: The explanation is that when Abd al-Qadir died, his share transferred to his three children: Ali, Amr, Latifa, with the male having twice the female share, so for Ali two-fifths, for Amr two-fifths, for Latifa one-fifth. This is the apparent view in our school. And it is possible that it is said that Abd al-Rahman and Malaka, the children of Muhammad who died during his father's life, share with them, taking the place of their father, so they would have two-sevenths, Ali two-sevenths, Amr two-sevenths, Latifa one-seventh. And although this is possible, it is preponderated against in our school because the basis has three points. First: that the waqf founder's intention is that no one from his offspring is deprived; this is weak because intentions are not considered if the wording does not indicate them. Second: including them in the ruling and making the order between every root and its branch, not between both layers entirely; this is possible but against the apparent. I once inclined to it in a waqf with a wording that required it, but I do not generalize it in every order. Third: reliance on the founder's statement: 'whoever from the people of the waqf dies before becoming entitled, his child takes his place.' This is strong, but it only holds if the deceased during his father's life is considered from the people of the waqf.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses a further branch of the maxim when dealing with conditional divorce and waqf. First, it clarifies that if a husband says 'You are divorced' without using 'fa' to connect a condition, it is considered immediate divorce, not conditional, contrary to a narration from Abu Yusuf. Then it presents a complex waqf case from al-Subki's fatwas, which al-Asyuti considered a branch of the same maxim of metaphorical interpretation. The waqf is structured in layers: self, children, grandchildren, etc., with rules for when a beneficiary dies with or without children. The specific problem involves calculating the shares after multiple deaths. Al-Subki provides a detailed arithmetical solution based on the apparent Hanafi view, where the share of a deceased beneficiary goes to his children, but not to grandchildren of a predeceased son. The case: Abd al-Qadir's share goes to his three children (Ali, Amr, Latifa). After subsequent deaths, Fatima (Latifa's daughter) dies without descendant. Her share goes to her paternal uncle Ali's line, resulting in Zaynab (Ali's daughter) receiving 27/60, while the grandchildren of the predeceased son Muhammad (Abd al-Rahman and Malaka) get 22/60 and 11/60 respectively, but these are from the original share of Abd al-Qadir? Actually the calculation shows that the alternative view is rejected. Al-Subki argues that including the grandchildren of a predeceased son is preponderated against because it relies on weak intentions or straying from the apparent wording. The maxim applied here is that the wording of the founder is given priority over inferred intentions, and metaphorical interpretation is only used when necessary to avoid nullification.

Key Terms
وقف (waqf): Endowment, as specified by the founder.
نسل (nasl): Descendants, offspring.
فريضة (farīḍah): Prescribed shares (like inheritance).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In al-Subki's waqf fatwa, what share does Zaynab (the daughter of Ali) eventually receive according to the apparent Hanafi view?
A) 22/60
B) 11/60
C) 27/60
D) 1/5

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf law, the founder's literal wording overrides inferred intentions; grandchildren of a predeceased beneficiary do not substitute unless the wording explicitly allows it.

Interpreting Waqf Conditions: Specific Over General
Critique of al-Subki's interpretation and a calculation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-khass muqaddam ala al-amm' to resolve apparent conflicts between general and specific clauses in waqf deeds and calculate the resulting distribution.

Core Idea
Specific conditions in a waqf override general orders of inheritance, ensuring both clauses are operative.

Waqf deeds often combine general inheritance orders (like 'then' for layers) with specific exceptions (like 'if a beneficiary dies before receiving, his share goes to his children'). How do we reconcile them? The Hanafi answer is clear: the specific qualifies the general. In this lesson, we examine a classic case where Imam al-Subki hesitated, but the author of Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair decisively applies the maxim. We will follow the reasoning and the subsequent calculation to see the principle in action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general and specific clauses
Start by isolating the general rule: the waqf stipulates a tartib (order) among layers using 'thumma' (then). Then find the specific condition: 'man mata qabla istihqaqih' (whoever died before entitlement) with the detail that they had not received any benefit.
The general rule is common; the specific clause is additional.
2
Recognize the conflict between them
Under the general rule, if a beneficiary in the first layer dies, their share should pass to others in the same layer, not directly to their children. But the specific condition says children inherit if the parent dies before entitlement. These two rules conflict.
If both are applied literally, they cannot both be fully satisfied.
3
Apply the maxim 'al-khass muqaddam ala al-amm'
The Hanafi rule is that the specific takes precedence over the general. Therefore, the specific condition overrides the general order. Children inherit from the deceased parent even if the layer is not extinct.
This is the correct application per the author.
4
Verify the reconciliation
The author shows that al-Subki's interpretation (restricting the specific condition to those who die before the layer closes) would render the condition meaningless. By giving effect to both, we combine the two clauses: the general order applies except when the specific condition triggers.
This avoids nullifying any part of the waqf deed.
5
Work through the calculation
The second part of the passage distributes shares step by step. Follow the transfers: after each death, apply the specific condition if applicable, otherwise fall back on the general order. The final division confirms the principle.
The calculation serves as a concrete demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يطلق عليه أنه من أهل الوقف ممنوع وما ذكره في تأويل قوله قبل استحقاقه خلاف الظاهر من اللفظ، وخلاف المتبادر إلى الأفهام، بل صريح كلام الواقف أنه أراد بأهل الوقف الذي مات قبل استحقاقه الذي لم يدخل في الاستحقاق بالكلية ولكنه بصدد أن يصير إليه. وقوله لشيء من منافع الوقف دليل قوي لذلك، فإنه نكرة في سياق الشرط وفي سياق كلام معناه النفي فيعم لأن المعنى : ولم يستحق شيئًا من منافع الوقف. وهذا صريح في ردّ التأويل الذي قاله ويؤيده أيضا قوله: استحق ما كان يستحقه المتوفى لو بقي حيًّا إلى أن يصير له شيء من منافع الوقف. فهذه الألفاظ كلها صريحة في أنه مات قبل الاستحقاق، وأيضًا لو كان المراد ما قاله السبكي، لاستغنى عنه بقوله أولاً على أن مات عن ولد عاد ما كان جاريًا عليه على ولده فإنه يُغني عنه ولا ينافي هذا اشتراطه الترتيب في الطبقات بثم، لأن ذلك عام خصصه هذا كما خصَّصه أيضًا قوله على أن من مات عن ولد إلى آخره؛ وأيضًا فإنَّا إذا عملنا بعموم اشتراط الترتيب لزم منه إلغاء هذا الكلام بالكلية ولا يعمل في صورة لأنه على هذا التقدير إنما يستحق عبد الرحمن وملكة لما استويا في الدرجة أخذا من قوله عاد على مَن في درجته فبقي قوله : ومَن مات قبل استحقاقه إلى آخره مهملاً لا يظهر له أثر في صورة بخلاف ما إذا أعملناه وخصَّصنا به عموم الترتيب، فإن فيه إعمالاً للكلامين وجمعا بينهما، وهذا أمر ينبغي أن يقطع به حينئذ.
•
فنقول : لما مات عبد القادر قسم نصيبه بين أولاده الثلاثة وولدي ولده أسباعا؛ لعبد الرحمن وملكة السبعان أثلاثا فلما مات عمرو عن غير نسل انتقل نصيبه إلى أخويه وولدي أخيه ليصير نصيب عبد القادر كله بينهم؛ لعلي خمسان، وللطيفة خمس، ولعبد الرحمن وملكة خمسان أثلاثا ولما توفيت لطيفة انتقل نصيبها بكماله لبنتها فاطمة ولما مات علي انتقل نصيبه بكماله لبنته زينب ولما توفي فاطمة بنت لطيفة والباقون في درجتها : زينب وعبد الرحمن وملكة قسم نصيبها بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين اعتبارًا بهم بنت ربع. فاجتمع لعبد الرحمن بموت عمرو خمس وثلث وبموت فاطمة نصف خمس، ولملكة بموت عمرو ثلثا خمس، وبموت فاطمة ربع خمس، فيقسم نصيب عبد القادر ستين جزءًا لزينب سبعة وعشرون وهي خمسان وربع خمس ولعبد الرحمن اثنان وعشرون وهي خمس ونصف خمس وثلث خمس، ولملكة أحد عشر وهي ثلثا خمس وربع فصح ما قاله السبكي لكن الفرق بعدم استحقاق عبد الرحمن وملكة والجزم حينئذ بصحة هذه القسمة والسبكي تردد فيها وجعلها من باب قسمة المشكوك في استحقاقه ونحن لا نتردد في ذلك.
It is not permissible to say that he is from the people of the waqf; this is forbidden. And what he mentioned in the interpretation of his statement 'before his entitlement' contradicts the apparent meaning of the wording and contradicts what immediately comes to mind. Rather, the explicit statement of the waqif is that by 'people of the waqf' he meant the one who died before his entitlement, who did not enter into entitlement at all but was on the verge of attaining it. And his statement 'for something of the benefits of the waqf' is strong evidence for that, for it is an indefinite noun in the context of a conditional and in the context of speech whose meaning is negation, so it is general, because the meaning is: and he did not receive any of the benefits of the waqf. This is explicit in rejecting the interpretation he gave, and is also supported by his statement: 'he is entitled to what the deceased would have been entitled to had he remained alive until something of the benefits of the waqf came to him.' All these expressions are explicit that he died before entitlement. And also, if the intended meaning were what al-Subki said, he would have sufficed with his first statement: 'that if he dies leaving a child, what was running for him reverts to his child,' for that suffices and does not contradict his stipulation of order in the layers by 'then', because that is general which this particularizes, just as his statement 'that if he dies leaving a child, etc.' also particularized it. And also, if we act according to the generality of the stipulation of order, it necessitates nullifying this statement entirely and it does not operate in any case, because on this estimation, 'Abd al-Rahman and Mulayka only become entitled because they are equal in rank, taken from his statement 'it reverts to whoever is in his rank,' so his statement 'and whoever died before his entitlement, etc.' remains disregarded, having no effect in any case. This is unlike when we operate it and particularize the generality of order with it, for then there is operation of both statements and combination between them. And this is a matter that should be decisively held at that point.
•
So we say: When 'Abd al-Qadir died, his share was divided among his three children and the two children of his grandchild in sevenths: for 'Abd al-Rahman and Mulayka the two sevenths in thirds. Then when 'Amr died without offspring, his share transferred to his two brothers and his brother's two children, so that the entire share of 'Abd al-Qadir becomes among them: for 'Ali two-fifths, for Latifa one-fifth, for 'Abd al-Rahman and Mulayka two-fifths in thirds. Then when Latifa died, her entire share transferred to her daughter Fatima. Then when 'Ali died, his entire share transferred to his daughter Zaynab. Then when Fatima, daughter of Latifa, died, and the remaining at her rank: Zaynab, 'Abd al-Rahman, and Mulayka, her share was divided among them, for the male the portion of two females, considering them based on her daughter one-fourth. So for 'Abd al-Rahman, by the death of 'Amr, one-fifth and one-third, and by the death of Fatima, half of one-fifth; for Mulayka, by the death of 'Amr, two-thirds of one-fifth, and by the death of Fatima, one-fourth of one-fifth. So the share of 'Abd al-Qadir is divided into sixty parts: for Zaynab twenty-seven, which is two-fifths and a quarter of a fifth; for 'Abd al-Rahman twenty-two, which is one-fifth, half of one-fifth, and one-third of one-fifth; and for Mulayka eleven, which is two-thirds of one-fifth and one-fourth. So what al-Subki said is correct, but the difference is in the non-entitlement of 'Abd al-Rahman and Mulayka, and the certainty then of the validity of this division. Al-Subki hesitated about it and made it from the category of division of what is doubted in its entitlement, but we do not hesitate about that.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage begins with a critical analysis of al-Subki's interpretation of a waqf condition. The author argues that the phrase 'man mata qabla istihqaqih' (whoever died before his entitlement) is clear in meaning: it refers to a beneficiary who dies before receiving any benefit from the waqf. This interpretation is supported by the wording 'li shay'in min manafi' al-waqf' for something of the benefits of the waqf, which is indefinite in a conditional/negative context, thus general. The author then demonstrates that if we adopt al-Subki's interpretation (that the condition applies only before the layer is closed), the specific condition would be rendered meaningless. Instead, the author insists on giving effect to both conditions: the general order (al-tartib) and the specific exception for those who die before entitlement, by making the exception override the general order in cases where they conflict. The second part of the passage contains a detailed calculation of shares in a hypothetical waqf case, following the preferred interpretation. It shows how the shares are distributed among the heirs step by step, culminating with a final division that yields specific fractions for each heir. The author concludes that al-Subki's calculation is numerically correct, but al-Subki hesitated to confirm the entitlement of 'Abd al-Rahman and Mulayka due to the uncertainty in the interpretation. The author, however, is certain that they are not entitled in this case because they had already received benefits before the others died. This passage exemplifies the principle that specific conditions qualify general ones in waqf deeds.

Key Terms
الترتيب (al-tartib): The order of inheritance among layers (batn) in a waqf, usually expressed with 'thumma' (then), meaning that the children of a deceased beneficiary do not inherit from their parent's share until the entire layer is extinct.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): Entitlement to receive benefits from the waqf. The text distinguishes between those who have already received some benefit (and thus are not covered by the exception) and those who died before receiving any.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It contradicts the apparent meaning of the waqif's words
B) It would render the specific condition meaningless
C) It violates the general principle of tartib
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
When a waqf contains both a general order and a specific condition, the specific condition overrides the general, ensuring all clauses are operative.

Resolving Conflicting Waqf Conditions: The Specific over the General
Al-Subki's fatwa on a waqf with conflicting conditions and the author's analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of al-khas over al-'amm to resolve conflicting conditions in a waqf deed and construct a fatwa that prioritizes the specific text.

Core Idea
When a waqf deed has conflicting conditions, the specific text takes precedence over the general — this is how the Hanafi mufti resolves apparent contradictions.

This lesson presents a classic fatwa scenario from al-Subki, where a waqf deed contains two conditions that seem to clash. Through his analysis, you will see how the mufti uses the legal maxim 'al-khas muqaddam 'ala al-'amm' to determine the correct distribution. This is not just a theoretical rule; it is a practical tool that the author says he has applied many times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions in the waqf deed
Read the passage and extract the two clauses: one about brothers inheriting from a sibling, and another about children standing in place of a parent who died before entitlement.
These appear in the first sentence of the fatwa question.
2
Classify each condition as specific (khas) or general (amm)
The clause naming 'brothers' is specific because it refers to a particular group. The clause about 'whoever dies before entitlement' is general because it applies to any beneficiary in any situation.
Al-Subki explicitly calls the brothers' clause 'specific' (khas) and the other 'general' (amm).
3
Identify the conflict
The conflict arises when a female beneficiary (Khadija) dies: the brother condition says her share goes to her brother, while the general condition would give it to her deceased brother's son (nephew) as a substitute.
The two conditions yield different heirs for the same share.
4
Apply the principle: al-khas muqaddam 'ala al-'amm
Since the specific condition (brothers) directly addresses this situation, it overrides the general condition. The mufti gives precedence to the specific, ruling that the brother inherits exclusively.
This is the core legal reasoning in the fatwa.
5
Determine the final ruling
The share of Khadija goes to her brother alone, excluding the nephew. Al-Subki states this is the more preponderant (rajih) view.
The author of the passage later summarises al-Subki's more detailed layer-by-layer distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل السبكي أيضًا عن رجل وقف وقفًا على حمزة ثم أولاده ثم أولادهم، وشرط إن من مات أولاده انتقل نصيبه إلى الباقين من إخوته ومن مات قبل استحقاقه لشيء من منافع الوقف وله ولد استحق ولده ما كان يستحقه المتوفى لو كان حيا، فمات حمزة وخلف ولدين وهما عماد الدين وخديجة وولد ولد مات أبوه في حياة والده وهو نجم الدين بن مؤيد الدين بن حمزة فأخذ الولدان نصيبهما، وولد الولد نصيب الذي لو كان أبوه حيا لأخذه، ثم ماتت خديجة. فهل يختص أخوها بالباقي أو يشاركه ولد أخيه نجم الدين؟ فأجاب بأنه تعارض فيه اللفظان فيحتمل المشاركة، ولكن الأرجح اختصاص الأخ ويرجحه أن التنصيص على الإخوة وعلى الباقين منهم كالخاص وقوله ومَن مات قبل الاستحقاق كالعام فيقدم الخاص على العام. (انتهى). هذا آخر ما أورده الأسيوطي رحمه الله في هذه المسألة، وأنا أذكر حاصل السؤال وحاصل جواب السبكي، وحاصل ما خالف فيه الأسيوطي، ثم أذكر بعده ما عندي في ذلك، وإنما أطلت فيها لكثرة وقوعها، وقد أفتيت فيها مرارًا.
،
أما حاصل السؤال : إن الواقف وقف على ذريته مرتبا بين البطون بثم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وشرط انتقال نصيب المتوفى عن ولد إليه وعن غير ولد إلى من هو في درجته وإن من مات قبل استحقاقه وله ولد قام ولده مقامه لو بقي حيا، فمات الواقف عن ولدين ثم مات أحدهما عن ثلاثة وولدي ابن لم يستحق، ثم مات اثنان الثلاثة عن ولدين ثم مات واحد عن غير نسل ثم مات أحد الولدين عن غير نسل.
،
وحاصل جواب السبكي أن ما خص المتوفَّى وهو النصف مقسوم بين أولاده الثلاثة ولا شيء لولدي ابنه المتوفّى في حياته. ومن مات من الثلاثة عن غير نسل ردَّ نصيبه إلى إخوته فيكون النصف بينهما. ومن مات عن ولد فنصيبه له ما دام أهل طبقة أبيه . ثم من مات بعدهم يقسم نصيبه بين جميع أولاد الأولاد بالسَّوِيَّة، فيدخل ولد المتوفى في حياة أبيه، فتنتقض القسمة بموت الطبقة الثانية ويزول الحجب عن ولدي المتوفى في حياة أبيه عملاً بقوله ثم على أولاد أولاده وإنه إنما يعمل بقوله من مات عن ولد انتقل نصيبه إلى ولده ما دام البطن الأول فمَن مات من أهل البطن الأول انتقل نصيبه إلى ولده ويقسم الربع على هذا، فإذا لم يبق أحد من البطن الأول تنتقض القسمة وتكون بينهم بالسَّوِيَّة فمَن مات من أهل الثاني عن ولد انتقل نصيبه إليه إلى أن ينقرض أهل تلك الطبقة فتنتقض القسمة ويقسم بينهم بالسَّوِيَّة. وهكذا يفعل في كل بطن.
Al-Subki was also asked about a man who set up a waqf upon Hamza, then his children, then their children, and stipulated that if his children die, his share transfers to the remaining ones among his brothers, and whoever dies before his entitlement to any of the benefits of the waqf and has a child, that child is entitled to what the deceased would have been entitled to had he been alive. Hamza died leaving two children: 'Imad al-Din and Khadija, and a grandchild whose father died in the lifetime of his own father, namely Najm al-Din ibn Mu'ayyid al-Din ibn Hamza. The two children took their shares, and the grandchild took the share that his father would have taken had he been alive. Then Khadija died. Is her brother the only one entitled to the remainder, or does the son of his brother Najm al-Din share with him? He answered that the two phrases conflict, so sharing is possible, but the more likely is that the brother is the sole recipient, and the preponderance is because the explicit mention of the brothers and the remaining among them is like the specific, and his statement 'and whoever died before entitlement' is like the general, so the specific is given precedence over the general. (End). This is the end of what al-Asyuti, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned on this issue. I will mention the summary of the question, the summary of al-Subki's answer, the summary of where al-Asyuti disagreed, and then I will mention after that what I have on this. I have lengthened it because of its frequent occurrence, and I have given fatwa on it many times.

As for the summary of the question: The waqif made a waqf upon his descendants, arranged among the layers with 'thumma', for the male the portion of two females, and stipulated that the share of the deceased who dies leaving a child goes to that child, and if he dies without a child, it goes to whoever is in his rank, and whoever dies before his entitlement and has a child, his child stands in his place as if he remained alive. The waqif died leaving two children, then one of them died leaving three children and two sons of a son who had not become entitled, then two of the three died leaving two children, then one died without offspring, then one of the two children died without offspring.

As for the summary of al-Subki's answer: That which was allotted to the deceased, which is half, is divided among his three children, and nothing for the two sons of his son who died in his lifetime. Whoever among the three dies without offspring, his share is returned to his brothers, so the half is between them. Whoever dies leaving a child, his share goes to that child as long as the people of his father's layer remain. Then whoever dies after them, his share is divided among all the children of the descendants equally, so the child of the one who died in his father's lifetime enters, then the division is nullified by the death of the second layer, and the blocking is removed from the two children of the one who died in his lifetime, acting on his statement 'then upon his children', and that his statement 'whoever dies leaving a child, his share transfers to his child' only operates as long as the first layer exists. So whoever dies from the people of the first layer, his share transfers to his child, and the quarter is divided on this basis. Then when no one remains from the first layer, the division is nullified and it is equally among them. Then whoever dies from the people of the second layer leaving a child, his share transfers to him until the people of that layer become extinct, then the division is nullified and divided equally among them. And so on for every layer.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents a fatwa case from al-Subki regarding a waqf with two potentially conflicting conditions: one condition specifies that when a beneficiary dies, his share goes to his brothers (if any), and another condition states that if a beneficiary dies before receiving any benefit but leaves a child, the child stands in his place. The question is whether the surviving brother of Khadija is exclusively entitled to her share or whether the son of his predeceased brother also shares. Al-Subki rules that the specific mention of brothers takes precedence over the general condition about those dying before entitlement, because the explicit naming of the brothers is like a specific text that overrides the general. The author then summarizes al-Subki's longer explanation of how the succession works layer by layer: within each layer, children inherit from parents who die; when a layer becomes extinct, the shares are redistributed equally among all descendants in the next layer. This demonstrates the mufti's method of resolving textual conflict by prioritizing specific over general. The author notes that the question is common and he has given fatwa on it many times, indicating the practical importance of this principle.

Key Terms
التنصيص (al-tansis): Explicit mention or specification in the waqf deed, considered as a specific text (al-khas).
الخاص (al-khas): The specific condition or text that applies to a particular situation, as opposed to the general (al-'amm).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In al-Subki's fatwa, why does he rule that the brother of Khadija exclusively inherits her share rather than sharing with the nephew?
A) Because the nephew's father died before entitlement
B) Because the condition about brothers is specific, while the condition about children of those who die before entitlement is general
C) Because Khadija had no children
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
In a waqf with conflicting conditions, the specific text (al-khas) takes precedence over the general (al-'amm), as demonstrated by al-Subki's fatwa.

The Principle of Specific Over General in Waqf Distribution
Fatwa analysis on conflicting conditions in endowment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qa'ida 'al-khass yuqaddam ala al-amm' to resolve conflicting conditions in a waqf endowment and evaluate the differing views of al-Subki and al-Asyuti on the entitlement of grandchildren of a predeceased son.

Core Idea
When waqf conditions conflict, the specific text overrides the general – a key maxim for fatwa in endowment disputes.

This lesson examines a classic fatwa from Imam al-Subki on a multi-generational waqf where two conditions clash. You will see how the Hanafi legal maxim 'the specific takes precedence over the general' resolves the conflict, and then explore al-Asyuti's partial disagreement, which shifts the distribution. This case frequently occurs in practice, so mastering it will sharpen your fatwa drafting skills.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting conditions
The endowment contains two conditions: one specifies that if a child dies, his share transfers to his siblings (specific), and another general condition that if someone dies before entitlement leaving a child, that child takes his place. These conflict when a child dies leaving both a sibling and a nephew (child of a predeceased sibling).
Focus on the wording: 'transfer to siblings' is more specific than 'whoever dies before entitlement'.
2
Apply the qawida
Using the maxim 'al-khass yuqaddam ala al-amm' (the specific is given precedence over the general), the specific condition about siblings overrides the general condition. Thus, the sibling exclusively inherits the deceased's share.
This is a textbook application of the Hanafi usul principle.
3
Trace al-Subki's ruling
Al-Subki ruled that the brother alone takes Khadija's share because the sibling condition is specific. He also detailed how the division reverses after each generation's extinction (intiqad al-qisma).
Note the structure: al-Subki first identifies the conflict, then applies the maxim.
4
Analyze al-Asyuti's objection
Al-Asyuti disagreed with al-Subki on one point: he argued that children of a son who died in the father's lifetime should not be excluded while the first generation remains. They should be entitled immediately, as if their father were alive.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school on the interpretation of the 'then' (thumma) condition.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The author of our text sides with al-Asyuti, stating his view is obligatory due to his evidence. Therefore, the operative ruling for fatwa is that grandchildren of a predeceased son are entitled to share with the first generation.
This shows that within the Hanafi tradition, later authorities can adopt a different view from earlier ones when the evidence is stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسُئِلَ السبكي أيضًا عن رجل وقف وقفًا على حمزة ثم أولاده ثم أولادهم، وشرط إن من مات أولاده انتقل نصيبه إلى الباقين من إخوته ومن مات قبل استحقاقه لشيء من منافع الوقف وله ولد استحق ولده ما كان يستحقه المتوفى لو كان حيا، فمات حمزة وخلف ولدين وهما عماد الدين وخديجة وولد ولد مات أبوه في حياة والده وهو نجم الدين بن مؤيد الدين بن حمزة فأخذ الولدان نصيبهما، وولد الولد نصيب الذي لو كان أبوه حيا لأخذه، ثم ماتت خديجة. فهل يختص أخوها بالباقي أو يشاركه ولد أخيه نجم الدين؟ فأجاب بأنه تعارض فيه اللفظان فيحتمل المشاركة، ولكن الأرجح اختصاص الأخ ويرجحه أن التنصيص على الإخوة وعلى الباقين منهم كالخاص وقوله ومَن مات قبل الاستحقاق كالعام فيقدم الخاص على العام. (انتهى). هذا آخر ما أورده الأسيوطي رحمه الله في هذه المسألة، وأنا أذكر حاصل السؤال وحاصل جواب السبكي، وحاصل ما خالف فيه الأسيوطي، ثم أذكر بعده ما عندي في ذلك، وإنما أطلت فيها لكثرة وقوعها، وقد أفتيت فيها مرارًا. أما حاصل السؤال: إن الواقف وقف على ذريته مرتبا بين البطون بثم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، وشرط انتقال نصيب المتوفى عن ولد إليه وعن غير ولد إلى من هو في درجته وإن من مات قبل استحقاقه وله ولد قام ولده مقامه لو بقي حيا، فمات الواقف عن ولدين ثم مات أحدهما عن ثلاثة وولدي ابن لم يستحق، ثم مات اثنان الثلاثة عن ولدين ثم مات واحد عن غير نسل ثم مات أحد الولدين عن غير من نسل. وحاصل جواب السبكي أن ما خص المتوفَّى وهو النصف مقسوم بين أولاده الثلاثة ولا شيء لولدي ابنه المتوفّى في حياته. ومن مات من الثلاثة عن غير نسل ردَّ نصيبه إلى إخوته فيكون النصف بينهما. ومن مات عن ولد فنصيبه له ما دام أهل طبقة أبيه . ثم من مات بعدهم يقسم نصيبه بين جميع أولاد الأولاد بالسَّوِيَّة، فيدخل ولد المتوفى في حياة أبيه، فتنتقض القسمة بموت الطبقة الثانية ويزول الحجب عن ولدي المتوفى في حياة أبيه عملاً بقوله ثم على أولاد أولاده وإنه إنما يعمل بقوله من مات عن ولد انتقل نصيبه إلى ولده ما دام البطن الأول فمَن مات من أهل البطن الأول انتقل نصيبه إلى ولده ويقسم الربع على هذا، فإذا لم يبق أحد من البطن الأول تنتقض القسمة وتكون بينهم بالسَّوِيَّة فمَن مات من أهل الثاني عن ولد انتقل نصيبه إليه إلى أن ينقرض أهل تلك الطبقة فتنتقض القسمة ويقسم بينهم بالسَّوِيَّة. وهكذا يفعل في كل بطن. وحاصل مخالفة الأسيوطي له في شيء واحد؛ وهو أن أولاد المتوفّى في حياة أبيه لا يحرمون مع بقاء الطبقة الأولى وأنهم يستحقون معهم، ووافقه على انتقاض القسمة قلت: أما مخالفته في أولاد المتوفى في حياة أبيه فواجبة، لما ذكره الأسيوطي، وأما قوله تنقض القسمة بعد انقراض كل بطن؛ فقد أفتى به بعض علماء العصر وعزوا ذلك إلى الخصاف، ولم يتنبهوا لما صوّره الخصاف وما صوّره السبكي . فأنا أذكر حاصل ما ذكره الخصاف بالاختصار وأبين ما بينهما من الفرق
Al-Subki was also asked about a man who endowed a waqf upon Hamza, then his children, then their children, and stipulated that if any of his children die, his share transfers to his remaining siblings, and if someone dies before entitlement to any of the waqf benefits and leaves a child, that child inherits what the deceased would have taken if alive. Hamza died leaving two sons, 'Imad al-Din and Khadija, and a son of a son whose father died during his father's lifetime, namely Najm al-Din ibn Mu'ayyid al-Din ibn Hamza. The two children took their shares, and the grandson took the share that his father would have taken if alive. Then Khadija died. Is her brother alone entitled to the remainder, or does the son of his brother Najm al-Din share? He answered that the two wordings conflict, so sharing is possible, but the more probable view is that the brother is exclusively entitled, supported by the fact that specifying the siblings and the survivors among them is like a specific text, and his statement 'whoever dies before entitlement' is general, so the specific is given precedence over the general. (End). This is the end of what al-Asyuti, may Allah have mercy on him, presented on this issue. I will mention the summary of the question and the summary of al-Subki's answer, and the summary of where al-Asyuti disagreed, then I will mention my own view on that. I have lengthened it because it occurs frequently, and I have given fatwas on it repeatedly. As for the summary of the question: The endower endowed upon his descendants arranged by generations with 'thumma', for the male the share of two females, and stipulated the transfer of the share of the deceased with offspring to him, and without offspring to those at his level, and that whoever dies before entitlement and leaves a child, his child takes his place as if he were alive. The endower died leaving two sons, then one of them died leaving three and two sons of a son who were not entitled, then two of the three died leaving two children, then one died without offspring, then one of the two children died without offspring. And the summary of al-Subki's answer: That which is specific to the deceased, i.e., half, is divided among his three children, and the two sons of his son who died in his lifetime get nothing. Whoever among the three dies without offspring returns his share to his siblings, so the half is between them. Whoever dies with offspring, his share goes to his child as long as the people of his father's generation remain. Then whoever dies after them, his share is divided equally among all children's children, including the child of the one who died in his father's lifetime. Thus the division is reversed upon the death of the second generation, and the barrier is removed from the children of the one who died in his father's lifetime, acting on his statement 'then upon the children of his children.' And indeed it is acted upon by his statement 'whoever dies leaving a child, his share transfers to his child' as long as the first generation exists. Whoever among the first generation dies, his share transfers to his child, and the quarter is divided accordingly. When no one remains from the first generation, the division is reversed and it is among them equally. Whoever among the second generation dies leaving a child, his share transfers to him until the people of that generation are extinguished, then the division is reversed and divided among them equally. And so on in every generation. The summary of al-Asyuti's disagreement with him in one thing: That the children of the one who died in his father's lifetime are not excluded while the first generation remains, and they are entitled with them. He agreed with him on the reversal of the division. I said: As for his disagreement concerning the children of the one who died in his father's lifetime, it is obligatory, due to what al-Asyuti mentioned. As for his statement that the division is reversed after the extinction of each generation, some contemporary scholars have given fatwa on that and attributed it to al-Khassaf, but they did not pay attention to what al-Khassaf illustrated and what al-Subki illustrated. So I will mention the summary of what al-Khassaf mentioned briefly and clarify the difference between them.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a detailed fatwa analysis. It begins with a question to Imam al-Subki about a complex waqf case involving multiple generations. The key legal principle is that when the endower's conditions conflict, the more specific text (al-khass) is given precedence over the general (al-amm). Al-Subki rules that the brother (Khadija's brother) exclusively inherits her share because the condition specifying siblings is specific, while the condition about children of the predeceased is general. Al-Asyuti partially disagrees, arguing that children of the predeceased should not be excluded while the first generation remains. The author then summarizes the case, al-Subki's detailed answer showing how shares are distributed across generations with reversals of division, and al-Asyuti's objection. The author affirms al-Asyuti's view on children of the predeceased, but criticizes the attribution to al-Khassaf of the reversal rule, promising to clarify the difference.

Key Terms
الخاص (al-khass): A text that is specific in its application, e.g., mentioning 'siblings' specifically.
العام (al-amm): A text that is general, e.g., 'whoever dies before entitlement and leaves a child, that child inherits.'
انتقاض القسمة (intiqad al-qisma): The reversal of the initial division of waqf shares when a generation becomes extinct, allowing equal distribution among the next generation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the waqf case discussed by al-Subki, why does the brother exclusively inherit Khadija's share rather than sharing with the nephew?
A) Because the nephew is not a valid heir in waqf.
B) Because the condition 'transfer to siblings' is specific and overrides the general condition about children of predeceased.
C) Because al-Asyuti disagreed with al-Subki.
D) Because the nephew died before entitlement.

Key Takeaway
In waqf distribution, when conditions conflict, the specific condition (khass) overrides the general (amm), and the preferred view is that children of a predeceased generation are not excluded while the first generation exists.

Seven Waqf Distribution Cases from al-Khassaf
List of illustrative waqf distribution scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-waqf 'ala hasab ma sharatahu al-waqif' to determine waqf distribution in seven distinct scenarios by parsing the founder's wording, generational priority, and inheritance conditions.

Core Idea
Endowment distribution follows the founder's exact wording; al-Khassaf's cases show how priority and inheritance clauses alter shares.

This passage presents seven classic scenarios from Imam al-Khassaf that illustrate how the Hanafi maxim 'the endowment is according to the condition of the endower' governs distribution among generations. Each scenario varies by a single condition—priority (tartib), inclusion of children of predeceased, or specification of classes—yielding different rulings. By parsing these cases, you will learn to extract the operative conditions and apply the maxim in similar contemporary waqf cases.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the founder's wording
Read each scenario carefully and note key phrases: does the founder use 'then' (thumma) for priority? Does he include conditions like 'whoever dies leaving a child' or specify 'my children and their children'?
The wording is decisive per the maxim.
2
2. Determine the generational hierarchy
Identify the generations (butun) mentioned: e.g., first generation (founder's children), second (grandchildren), third (great-grandchildren). Note whether priority is stated or absent.
Without priority, all generations share equally; with priority, lower generations are blocked until higher are extinct.
3
3. Apply the maxim to derive the ruling
Apply the maxim 'the endowment is according to the condition of the endower' to the wording. If the founder said 'then', the higher generation takes first. If he included a condition about death with children, that condition is binding.
The ruling is simply the logical consequence of the wording.
4
4. Verify the applicability of intiqad al-qisma
Check if the division reverses (intiqad) upon extinction of a generation. This happens only when shares are calculated yearly based on living beneficiaries, not when priority blocks generations permanently.
Intiqad is a mechanism to maintain equality among equal beneficiaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فذكر الخصاف صورًا : الأولى : وقف على ذريته بلا ترتيب بين البطون استحق الجميع بالسوية؛ الأعلى والأسفل، فتنتقض القسمة في كل سنة بحسب قلتهم وكثرتهم. الثانية : وقف عليهم شارطًا تقديم البطن الأعلى ثم وثم ولم يزد، فلا شيء لأهل البطن الثاني ما دام واحد من الأعلى ومَن مات عن ولد فلا شيء لولده، ويستحق من مات أبوه قبل الاستحقاق مع أهل البطن الثاني لا مع الأول لكونه منهم. الثالثة : وقف على ولده وأولادهم ونسلهم لا يدخل ولد من كان أبوه مات قبل الوقف، لكونه خصص أولاد الولد الموقوف عليه فخرج المتوفى قبله . الرابعة : وقف على أولاده وأولاد أولاده وذريته على أن يبدأ بالبطن الأعلى ثم وثم . قلنا لا شيء للبطن الثاني ما دام واحد من الأعلى، فلو مات واحد من البطن الثاني وترك ولدًا مع وجود الأعلى ثم انقرض الأعلى فلا مشاركة له مع البطن الثاني من الثالث، فإذا انقرض الثاني شارك الثالث. الخامسة : وقف على أولاده وأولاد أولاده وذريته ونسله ولم يرتب، وشرط أن من مات عن ولد فنصيبه له وحكمه قسمة الغلة بين الولد وولد الوالد بالسوية، فما أصاب المتوفَّى كان لولده فيكون لهذا الولد سهمان؛ سهمه المجعول له معهم بالسَّوِيَّة وما انتقل إليه من والده . السادسة: وقف على ولده لصلبه ذكرًا وأُنثى وعلى أولاد الذكور من ولده وأولاد أولادهم ونسلهم وحكمه قسمة الغلة بين ولده ذكرًا وأنثى وأولاد الذكور ذكرًا وأنثى بالسوية، فيدخل أولاد بنات البنين، فلو قال بعده يقدم الأعلى ثم وثم؛ اختص ولده لصلبه ذكرًا وأُنثى، فإذا انقرضوا صار لولد البنين دون أولاد البنات ثم لأولاد هؤلاء أبدا. السابعة وقف على بناته وأولادهن وأولاد أولادهن . وحكمه أن الغلة لبناته ونسلهن. فلو قال يقدم البطن الأعلى اتبع، فإن شرط بعد انقراضهن ونسلهن لولده
Then al-Khassaf mentioned scenarios: First: An endowment upon his descendants without ordering between generations; all are entitled equally, higher and lower; the division is reversed each year according to their decrease and increase. Second: An endowment upon them with the condition of giving precedence to the higher generation, then and then, without adding more; so nothing goes to the people of the second generation as long as one of the higher generation remains, and whoever dies leaving a child, nothing goes to his child, and the one whose father died before entitlement is entitled with the people of the second generation, not with the first, because he is from them. Third: An endowment upon his children and their children and their descendants; the child of one whose father died before the endowment does not enter, because he specified the children of the endowed child, so the one who died before it is excluded. Fourth: An endowment upon his children and his children's children and his descendants, with the condition that the highest generation begins, then and then. We say: nothing for the second generation as long as one of the higher generation remains. If one of the second generation dies leaving a child while the higher generation exists, then the higher generation becomes extinct, that child has no participation with the second generation from the third; when the second becomes extinct, the third participates. Fifth: An endowment upon his children and his children's children and his descendants and offspring without ordering, and he stipulated that whoever dies leaving a child, his share goes to him; its ruling is that the revenue is divided between the child and the parent of the child equally. What strikes the deceased is for his child, so this child has two shares: his share assigned to him equally with them, and what transferred to him from his father. Sixth: An endowment upon his own children, male and female, and upon the sons' children from his children and their children and their descendants; its ruling is that the revenue is divided between his children, male and female, and the sons' children, male and female, equally. Children of daughters of sons enter. If he says afterward 'give precedence to the highest, then and then', then it is specific to his own children, male and female; when they become extinct, it becomes for the sons' children without the daughters' children, then for their children forever. Seventh: An endowment upon his daughters and their children and their children's children. Its ruling is that the revenue is for his daughters and their descendants. If he says 'give precedence to the highest generation', it is followed. If he stipulates after their extinction and their descendants, for his child...

Commentary
This passage presents seven distinct scenarios (suwar) from Imam al-Khassaf that illustrate the rules of waqf distribution among generations. Each scenario varies based on the wording of the endowment: whether there is priority ordering (tartib), whether conditions about death with or without children are included, and whether the endowment includes specific relatives. The rulings show when shares are distributed equally, when generational precedence blocks lower generations, when children of predeceased parents are included or excluded, and how the division reverses (intiqad al-qisma) upon extinction of a generation. These cases are classic furu' (applications) of the broader principles governing waqf conditions.

Key Terms
البطن (al-batn): A generation or tier of beneficiaries in a waqf hierarchy.
انتقاض القسمة (intiqad al-qisma): The recalculation and redistribution of waqf shares when a generation becomes extinct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

In al-Khassaf's second scenario (وقف عليهم شارطًا تقديم البطن الأعلى ثم وثم ولم يزد), what happens to the share of a person from the second generation who dies leaving a child while someone from the first generation is still alive?
A) The child inherits the share immediately.
B) The share goes back to the first generation.
C) The share is given to the child only after the first generation becomes extinct.
D) The share is divided among all members of the second generation.

Key Takeaway
The endowment follows the founder's conditions; al-Khassaf's seven scenarios show how priority, inheritance clauses, and wording affect generational distribution.
