Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
15 min
Waqf Distribution Scenarios from al-Khassaf
Eight waqf distribution cases rule on deceased beneficiaries' shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'Whoever dies leaving a child, his share goes to his child' to waqf distribution scenarios and identify its exceptions when the waqif stipulates order.

Core Idea
A deceased beneficiary's share passes to his child only if the waqif so stipulates or the waqf is unordered.

This lesson examines eight waqf distribution scenarios from the Hanafi jurist al-Khassaf. At the heart of these scenarios is a key legal maxim: the share of a deceased beneficiary transfers to his children. However, this transfer is not automatic—it depends on the waqif's conditions regarding class precedence (al-tartib) and explicit transmission clauses. The scenarios illustrate the interplay between the general principle and its exceptions, providing a practical guide for muftis calculating waqf income distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing qawida
Recognize the general principle: the share of a deceased beneficiary transfers to his children unless the waqif conditioned otherwise.
The maxim is: 'من مات عن ولد فنصيبه لولده'.
2
Distinguish between ordered and unordered waqf
Determine if the waqif stipulated precedence (tartib) among generations. Without order, all share equally, and the transfer rule applies freely. With order, lower classes are excluded while the higher class exists.
Order suspends entitlement of lower classes.
3
Apply the transmission condition
Check if the waqif explicitly stated that the share of a deceased goes to his child. If so, the child takes even if a higher class exists; if not, the child gets nothing while the higher class remains.
This condition overrides the normal exclusion under order.
4
Determine the effect of a beneficiary's death before the waqf
If a person died before the waqf was established, his children do not enter as beneficiaries unless the waqif explicitly included them.
The waqf is established on existing beneficiaries.
5
Calculate shares when multiple deaths occur within and across classes
Apply arithmetic division based on the number of living beneficiaries and the shares of deceased that pass to their children. Each child takes the father's portion.
Recalculation may be annual or upon extinction of a class.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل مخالفة الأسيوطي له في شيء واحد ؛ وهو أن أولاد المتوفّى في حياة أبيه. لا يحرمون مع بقاء الطبقة الأولى وأنهم يستحقون معهم، ووافقه على انتقاض القسمة قلت: أما مخالفته في أولاد المتوفى في حياة أبيه فواجبة، لما ذكره الأسيوطي، وأما قوله تنقض القسمة بعد انقراض كل بطن ؛ فقد أفتى به بعض علماء العصر وعزوا ذلك إلى الخصاف، ولم يتنبهوا لما صوّره الخصاف وما صوّره السبكي . فأنا أذكر حاصل ما ذكره الخصاف بالاختصار وأبين ما بينهما من الفرق فذكر الخصاف صورًا : الأولى : وقف على ذريته بلا ترتيب بين البطون استحق الجميع بالسوية؛ الأعلى والأسفل، فتنتقض القسمة في كل سنة بحسب قلتهم وكثرتهم. الثانية : وقف عليهم شارطًا تقديم البطن الأعلى ثم وثم ولم يزد، فلا شيء لأهل البطن الثاني ما دام واحد من الأعلى ومَن مات عن ولد فلا شيء لولده، ويستحق من مات أبوه قبل الاستحقاق مع أهل البطن الثاني لا مع الأول لكونه منهم. الثالثة : وقف على ولده وأولادهم ونسلهم لا يدخل ولد من كان أبوه مات قبل الوقف، لكونه خصص أولاد الولد الموقوف عليه فخرج المتوفى قبله . الرابعة : وقف على أولاده وأولاد أولاده وذريته على أن يبدأ بالبطن الأعلى ثم وثم . قلنا لا شيء للبطن الثاني ما دام واحد من الأعلى، فلو مات واحد من البطن الثاني وترك ولدًا مع وجود الأعلى ثم انقرض الأعلى فلا مشاركة له مع البطن الثاني من الثالث، فإذا انقرض الثاني شارك الثالث. الخامسة : وقف على أولاده وأولاد أولاده وذريته ونسله ولم يرتب، وشرط أن من مات عن ولد فنصيبه له وحكمه قسمة الغلة بين الولد وولد الوالد بالسوية، فما أصاب المتوفَّى كان لولده فيكون لهذا الولد سهمان؛ سهمه المجعول له معهم بالسَّوِيَّة وما انتقل إليه من والده . السادسة: وقف على ولده لصلبه ذكرًا وأُنثى وعلى أولاد الذكور من ولده وأولاد أولادهم ونسلهم وحكمه قسمة الغلة بين ولده ذكرًا وأنثى وأولاد الذكور ذكرًا وأنثى بالسوية، فيدخل أولاد بنات البنين، فلو قال بعده يقدم الأعلى ثم وثم؛ اختص ولده لصلبه ذكرًا وأُنثى، فإذا انقرضوا صار لولد البنين دون أولاد البنات ثم لأولاد هؤلاء أبدا. السابعة وقف على بناته وأولادهن وأولاد أولادهن . وحكمه أن الغلة لبناته ونسلهن. فلو قال يقدم البطن الأعلى اتبع، فإن شرط بعد انقراضهن ونسلهن لولده الذكور ونسلهنَّ اتَّبع، فإن مات بعض ولده الذكور عن أولاد وبقي البعض ولهم أولاد، وحكمه عند عدم الترتيب أن الغلة لهم سواء، فإن رئب فالغلة للباقين من ولده فإذا انقرضوا كانت لولد المتوفى . الثامنة : وقف على ولده وولد ولده ونسلهم مرتبا شارطًا أن من مات عن ولد فنصيبه له وعن غير ولد فراجع إلى الوقف وحكمه أن الغلة للأعلى ثم وثم. فإن قسمت سنين ثم مات بعضهم عن نسل. قال تقسم على عدد أولاد الواقف الموجودين يوم الوقف، وعلى أولاده الحادثين له بعده؛ فما أصاب الأحياء أخذوه وما أصاب الميت كان لولده، وإنما جعل لولد من مات حصة أبيه مع وجود البطن الأعلى مع كون الواقف شرط تقديم الأعلى لكونه قال بعده إن من مات عن ولد فنصيبه له، وكذا لو مات الأعلى إلا واحدًا فيجعل سهم الميت لابنه وإن كان من البطن الثالث مع وجود الأعلى ولو كان عدد البطن الأعلى عشرة فمات اثنان بلا ولد ونسل، ثم مات آخران عن ولد لكل ثم مات آخران عن غير ولد. وحكمه تقسم الغلة على ستة؛ على هؤلاء الأربعة وعلى الميتين اللذين تركا أولادًا فما أصاب الأربعة فهو لهم وما أصاب الميتين كان لأولادهما، ولو مات واحد من العشرة عن ولد ثم مات ثمانية عن غير نسل تقسم على سهمين؛ سهم للحي للميت يكون لأولاده، فلو قسمناها سنين بين الأعلى وهم عشرة ثم مات اثنان عن غير ولد ثم مات واحد عن أربعة أولاد وواحد عن أولاد، ثم مات من الأربعة واحد وترك ولدا ومات آخر عن غير ولد، تقسم الغلة على ثمانية. فما أصاب الأحياء أخذوه وما أصاب الموتى كان لأولادهم لكل سهم أبيه، ثم ينظر إلى ما أصاب الأربعة يقسم أرباعًا فيرد سهم من مات عن غير ولد إلى أصل الوقف فتُعاد القسمة على ثمانية؛ فما أصاب والدهم قسم بين الاثنين الباقيين وبين أخيهم الميت الذي مات عن ولد أثلاثا فما أصاب الميت كان لولده، فلو لم يمت أحد من البطن الأعلى ومات واحد من الثاني عن ولد أو مات بعض الأعلى ثم من الثاني رجل أو رجلان عن ولد وحكمه أنه لا شيء لولد من مات قبل أبيه ولا لأولاد من مات من الثاني لعدم استحقاق الأب. ثم أعاد الإمام الخصاف رحمه الله الصورة الثامنة من غير زيادة ولا نقص وفرع أن البطن الأعلى لو كانوا عشرة وكان لهم ابنان ماتا قبل الوقف وترك كل ولدا، لا حق لهما ما دام واحد من الأعلى لأنهما من البطن الثاني فلا حق لهما حتى ينقرض الأول، فلو مات العشرة وترك كل ولدًا أخذ كل نصيب أبيه ولا شيء
The summary of al-Asyuti's opposition to him in one thing: that the children of one who died during his father's lifetime are not deprived as long as the first class remains, but they deserve with them. And he agreed with him on the annulment of the division. I said: As for his opposition regarding the children of one who died during his father's lifetime, it is obligatory, due to what al-Asyuti mentioned. As for his statement that the division is annulled after the extinction of each class, some scholars of the age gave fatwa on that and attributed it to al-Khassaf, and they did not pay attention to what al-Khassaf depicted and what al-Subki depicted. So I will mention the summary of what al-Khassaf mentioned concisely and clarify the difference between them. Al-Khassaf mentioned scenarios: First: Waqf on his descendants without order among classes; all deserve equally, the higher and the lower, so the division is annulled every year according to their fewness and numerousness. Second: Waqf on them with the condition of giving precedence to the highest class, then the next, and not more; so nothing for the people of the second class as long as one from the highest remains, and whoever dies leaving a child, nothing for his child. And one whose father died before entitlement deserves with the people of the second class, not with the first, because he is among them. Third: Waqf on his children and their children and their descendants; the child of one whose father died before the waqf does not enter, because he specified the children of the child of the waqif, so one who died before it is excluded. Fourth: Waqf on his children and his children's children and his descendants, with the condition to start with the highest class then the next. We say: nothing for the second class as long as one from the highest remains. If one from the second class dies leaving a child while the highest exists, then the highest becomes extinct, he does not participate with the second class from the third; when the second becomes extinct, the third participates. Fifth: Waqf on his children and his children's children and his descendants and his progeny without ordering, and he stipulated that whoever dies leaving a child, his share is for him; its ruling is dividing the produce between the child and the child's parent equally. What befalls the deceased is for his child, so this child has two shares: his share assigned to him with them equally, and what transferred to him from his father. Sixth: Waqf on his own children, male and female, and on the male children of his children and their children's children and their descendants. Its ruling is dividing the produce between his children, male and female, and the male children of his children, male and female, equally. So the children of granddaughters enter. If he then says: give precedence to the highest then the next, his own children, male and female, are exclusive. When they become extinct, it goes to the children of sons, not the children of daughters, then to the children of these forever. Seventh: Waqf on his daughters and their children and the children of their children. Its ruling is that the produce is for his daughters and their offspring. If he says give precedence to the highest class, it is followed. If he stipulates after their extinction and their offspring for his male children and their offspring, it is followed. If some of his male children die leaving children and some remain and have children, its ruling in the absence of ordering is that the produce is for them equally. If a share is distributed, the produce is for the remaining of his children; when they become extinct, it is for the children of the deceased. Eighth: Waqf on his children and his children's children and their descendants, ordered, stipulating that whoever dies leaving a child, his share is for him, and leaving no child, it returns to the waqf. Its ruling is that the produce is for the highest then the next. If it is divided for years then some of them die leaving offspring, he said: it is divided according to the number of the waqif's children existing on the day of the waqf and his children born after it. What befalls the living they take, and what befalls the deceased is for his child. He made the share of the deceased father for his child despite the presence of the highest class, because the waqif stipulated after that whoever dies leaving a child, his share is for him. Similarly, if the highest class dies except one, the share of the deceased is given to his son even if he is from the third class while the highest exists. If the number of the highest class was ten, two die without child or offspring, then two die leaving children each, then two more die without children. Its ruling: the produce is divided into six: upon these four and upon the two deceased who left children. What befell the four is for them, and what befell the two deceased is for their children. If one of the ten dies leaving a child, then eight die without offspring, it is divided into two shares: a share for the living, and a share for the deceased to be for his children. If we divide it over years among the highest who are ten, then two die without children, then one dies leaving four children and one leaving children, then one of the four dies leaving a child and another dies without child, the produce is divided into eight. What befalls the living they take, and what befalls the dead is for their children, each taking the share of his father. Then looking at what befalls the four, it is divided into quarters; the share of one who dies without child is returned to the origin of the waqf, so the division is repeated into eight. What befalls their father is divided among the two remaining and their brother who died leaving children, into thirds; what befalls the deceased is for his child. If no one of the highest class dies and one of the second dies leaving a child, or some of the highest die then from the second a man or two men die leaving children, its ruling: there is nothing for the child of one who died before his father nor for the children of one who died from the second, due to the lack of entitlement of the father. Then Imam al-Khassaf repeated the eighth scenario without addition or subtraction and elaborated: if the highest class were ten and they had two sons who died before the waqf, each leaving a child, they have no right as long as one of the highest remains, because they are from the second class; they have no right until the first becomes extinct. If the ten die each leaving a child, each takes the share of his father and nothing
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of eight waqf distribution scenarios from al-Khassaf, illustrating the application of the rule that the share of a deceased beneficiary passes to his children, subject to the conditions set by the waqif. The passage begins by noting al-Asyuti's opposition to a view that children of a person who died in his father's lifetime are not deprived; al-Asyuti holds they deserve alongside the first class. The author explains that al-Asyuti's view is correct. Then it addresses a fatwa attributed to al-Khassaf that division is annulled after the extinction of each class; the author clarifies the difference between al-Khassaf's and al-Subki's depictions. The eight scenarios cover various conditions: without order, with order stipulating precedence, exclusion of those whose fathers died before the waqf, the effect of dying with or without children, and the complex calculations when generations intermingle. Key rulings include: in an unordered waqf, all descendants share equally and division varies yearly with numbers; in an ordered waqf, the highest class excludes lower classes until extinct; a condition 'whoever dies leaving a child, his share goes to his child' creates inheritance-like transfer; and detailed calculation methods for splitting shares after deaths. The eighth scenario is particularly intricate, showing how to compute shares for living and deceased. The passage demonstrates that the waqif's stipulations regarding order and transmission of shares are paramount, and the jurist applies arithmetic division accordingly.

Key Terms
البطن (al-batn): A generation or class of descendants in a waqf (e.g., children, grandchildren).
الغلة (al-ghulla): The produce or revenue generated by the waqf property, to be distributed among beneficiaries.
الترتيب (al-tartib): Condition in waqf that gives precedence to one class over another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In scenario 2, if the waqif stipulates 'give precedence to the highest class then the next' without additional condition, what happens to the share of a person from the second class who dies leaving a child while a member of the highest class is still alive?
A) The child receives the father's share immediately.
B) The child receives nothing as long as the highest class exists.
C) The share goes to the highest class.
D) The child receives a portion equal to his father's share.

Key Takeaway
The transfer of a deceased waqf beneficiary's share to his children is governed by the waqif's stipulations: without a transmission clause, children are excluded while a higher class exists; with it, they take immediately.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
advanced
12 min
The Later Abrogates the Earlier: Conflicting Conditions in Waqf Division
Expanding the rule of overturning division via wording and maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-akhir nasikh li'l-awwal' to determine whether to overturn waqf division (naqd al-qisma) when the upper generation dies out, based on whether the waqif used 'wa' or 'thumma' between generations.

Core Idea
In waqf, the later condition abrogates the earlier; thus, overturning division depends on wording: 'wa' grants shares from start, 'thumma' delays them.

The division of waqf proceeds among generations is not static. When the upper generation of beneficiaries dies out, a question arises: should we redistribute the shares among the next generation, or continue the original plan as if the deceased's shares go to their descendants? The answer hinges on the precise wording used by the waqif. In this lesson, we unpack the Hanafi position that later conditions override earlier ones, and examine how the conjunctions 'wa' and 'thumma' lead to opposite rulings on overturning the division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions in the waqf deed
The waqf typically contains two key stipulations: (1) the beneficiaries are described as 'children and children's children', and (2) a condition that 'whoever dies leaving a child, his share goes to his child'. These may conflict when the upper stratum dies out.
The first condition appears at the start of the deed; the second is usually a subsequent clause.
2
Determine the wording between generations
Check whether the waqif joined 'children' and 'grandchildren' with 'wa' (and) or 'thumma' (then). 'Wa' implies simultaneous inclusion; 'thumma' implies sequential exclusion.
This is the decisive factor for naqd al-qisma.
3
Apply the maxim: later condition abrogates earlier
If the two conditions conflict, the Hanafi rule is that the later stipulation overrides the earlier. For example, if the deed first forbids sale but later permits substitution, the later permission prevails.
This maxim is drawn from al-Khassaf's ruling on substitution.
4
Analyze the interaction with wording
When 'wa' is used, the first condition (inclusive of grandchildren) is earlier, and the death-transfer clause is later. However, the later clause does not abrogate the earlier inclusion because it does not directly contradict it—instead it adds detail. But when the upper stratum dies, the earlier inclusion demands equal division among all grandchildren, overriding the death-transfer for individual shares. Thus, overturning occurs.
When 'thumma' is used, grandchildren are not initially included, so the death-transfer clause stands alone and continues after extinction of the upper stratum—no overturning.
5
Note the cross-madhab dispute
Al-Subki (Shafi'i) held that in case of conflict, the earlier condition prevails. This would lead to different results in the substitution example and potentially in the waqf division. The Hanafi position is relied upon for fatwa.
The dispute is rooted in interpretation of the maxim 'shart al-waqif ka-nass al-shari'.

لولد من مات قبل الواقف، وإن استووا في الطبقة فإن بقي منهم واحد قسمت على عشرة فما أصاب الحيّ أخذه وما أصاب الموتى كان لأولادهم، فإن مات العاشر عن ولد انتقلت القسمة لانقراض البطن الأعلى ورجعت إلى البطن الثاني فينظر إلى أولاد العشرة وأولاد الميت قبل الوقف فيقسم بالسَّوِيَّة بينهم، ولا يردّ نصيب من مات إلى ولده إلا قبل انقراض البطن الأعلى فيقسم على عدد البطن الأعلى فما أصاب الميت كان لولده فإذا انقرض البطن الأعلى نقضنا القسمة وجعلناها على عدد البطن الثاني ولم نعمل باشتراط انتقال نصيب الميت إلى ولده هنا لكون الواقف قال على ولده وولد ولده فلزم دخول أولاد من مات قبل الوقف فلزم نقض القسمة، فلو لم يكن له ولد إلا العشرة فماتوا واحدًا بعد واحد وكلما مات واحد ترك أولادًا حتى مات العشرة؛ فمنهم من ترك خمسة أولاد، ومنهم من ترك ثلاثة أولاد، ومنهم من ترك ستة أولاد ومنهم من ترك واحدا. أليس قلت فمن مات كان نصيبه لولده؟ فلما مات العاشر كيف تقسم الغلة؟ قال أنقض القسمة الأولى وأردّ ذلك إلى عدد البطن الثاني فأنظر جماعتهم فأقسمها على عددهم. ويبطل قوله من مات عن ولد انتقل نصيبه لولده لأن الأمر يؤول إلى قوله ولد ولدي. وكذلك لو مات جميع ولد ولد الصلب ولم يبق منهم أحد؛ فنظرنا إلى البطن الثالث فوجدناهم ثمانية أنفس وكذلك كل بطن يصير لهم فإنما تقسم على عددهم ويبطل ما كان قبل ذلك (انتهى) . فأخذ بعض العصريين من الصورة الثامنة، وبيان حكمها أن الخصاف قائل بنقض القسمة في مثل مسألة السبكي ولم يتأمل الفرق بين الصورتين فإن في مسألة السبكي؛ وقف على أولاده ثم أولادهم بكلمة ثم بين الطبقتين. وفي مسألة الخصاف؛ وقف على ولده وولد ولده بالواو لا بثم، فصدر مسألة الخصاف اقتضى اشتراك البطن الأعلى على السفلى، وصدر مسألة السبكي اقتضى عدم الاشتراك فالقول بنقض القسمة وعدمه مبني على هذا. والدليل عليه أن الخصاف بعدما قرر نقض القسمة كما ذكرناه قال : فإن قلت : فلمَ كان هذا القول عندك المعمول به وتركت قوله كلما حدث على أحد منهم الموت كان نصيبه مردودًا إلى ولده وولد ولده ونسله أبدا ما تناسلوا؟ قلت : من قبل أنَّا بعضهم يدخل في الغلة ويجب حقه فيها بنفسه لا بأبيه فعملنا بذلك وقسمنا الغلة على عددهم. (انتهى). فقد أفاد أن سبب نقضها دخول ولد الولد مع الولد بصدر الكلام، فإذا كان صدره لا يتناول ولد الولد مع الولد بل مخرج له كيف يقال بنقض القسمة؟ فإن قلت: قد صدقت أن الخصاف صورها بالواو ولكن ذكر بعده ما يفيد معنى ثم، وهو تقديم البطن الأعلى فاستويا قلت نعم لكن هو إخراج بعد الدخول في الأول بخلاف التعبير بثم من أول الكلام فإن البطن الثاني لم يدخل مع البطن الأول، فكيف يصح أن يستدلّ بكلام الخصاف على مسألة السبكي مع أن السبكي بنى القول بنقض القسمة على أن الواقف إذا ذكر شرطين متعارضين يعمل بأولهما؟ قال: وليس هذا النسخ حتى يعمل بالمتأخر فإن كان هذا رأي السبكي في الشرطين، فلا كلام في عدم التعويل عليه وإن كان مذهب الشافعي رحمه الله فهو مشكل على قولهم : إن شرط الواقف كنص الشارع فإنه يقتضي العمل بالمتأخر، وحيث كان مبنى كلام السبكي على ذلك لم يصح القول به على مذهبنا؛ فإن مذهبنا العمل بالمتأخر منهما. قال الإمام الخصاف إنه لو كتب في أول المكتوب بعد الوقف : لا يُباع ولا يوهب وكتب في آخره : على أن لفلان ذلك والاستبدال بثمنه، كان له الاستبدال . قال من قبل إن الآخر ناسخ للأول ولو كان على عكسه امتنع بيعه . (انتهى). فالحاصل أن الواقف إذا وقف على أولاده وأولاد أولاده، وعلى أولاد أولاد أولاده، وعلى ذريته ونسله طبقة بعد طبقة وبطنا بعد بطن يحجب الطبقة العليا السفلى. على أن من مات عن ولد انتقل إلى ولده ومن مات عن غير ولد انتقل نصيبه إلى من هو في درجته وذوي طبقته وعلى أن من مات قبل دخوله في هذا الوقف واستحقاقه لشيء من منافعه وترك ولدا أو ولد ولد أو أسفل من ذلك استحق ما كان يستحقه أبوه لو كان حيا. بيع هذه الصورة كثيرة الوقوع بالقاهرة ولكن بعضهم يعبر عنها بثم بين الطبقات، وبعضهم بالواو، فإن كان بالواو يقسم الوقف بين الطبقة العليا وبين أولاد المتوفى في حياة الواقف قبل دخوله؛ فلهم ما خص آباءهم لو كان حيًا مع إخوته. فمن مات من أولاد الواقف وله ولد كان نصيبه لولده ومَن مات عن غير ولد كان نصيبه لإخوته، فيستمر الحال كذلك إلى انقراض البطن الأعلى وهي مسألة الخصاف التي قال فيها بنقض القسمة حيث ذكر بالواو، وقد علمته. وإن ذكر بثم، فمن مات عن ولد من أهل البطن الأول انتقل نصيبه إلى ولده ويستمر له ولا ينقض أصلاً بعده ولو انقرض أهل البطن الأول، فإذا مات أحد ولدي الواقف عن ولد الآخر عن عشرة كان النصف لولد من مات وله ولد والنصف الآخر للعشرة، فإذا مات ابنا الواقف استمر النصف للواحد والنصف للعشرة، وإن استووا في الطبقة؛ فقوله على أن من مات وله ولد مخصوص من ترتيب البطون فلا يُراعى الترتيب فيه. ثم من كان له شيء ينتقل إلى ولده، وهكذا إلى آخر البطون، حتى لو قدر أن الواقف مات عن ولدين ثم إن
For the child of the one who died before the waqif, and if they are equal in the layer, if one of them remains, it is divided into ten; what reaches the living he takes, and what reaches the dead goes to their children. If the tenth dies leaving a child, the division is transferred due to the extinction of the upper stratum and returns to the second stratum; then we look at the children of the ten and the children of the one who died before the waqf and divide equally among them. The share of the deceased is not returned to his child except before the extinction of the upper stratum; then it is divided according to the number of the upper stratum, and what reaches the deceased goes to his child. When the upper stratum becomes extinct, we overturn the division and make it according to the number of the second stratum, and we do not act on the stipulation that the share of the deceased transfers to his child here because the waqif said 'on his children and the children of his children', so the children of those who died before the waqf necessarily enter, so overturning the division becomes necessary. If he had no children except the ten, and they died one after another, each leaving children until all ten died; some left five children, some three, some six, some one. Did I not say that whoever dies, his share goes to his child? When the tenth dies, how do you divide the produce? He said: I overturn the first division and return to the number of the second stratum, look at their total, and divide among them. And his statement that whoever dies leaving a child, his share transfers to his child becomes void, because the matter ultimately leads to his statement 'children of my children'. Likewise, if all the children of the son of the loins died and none remains, we look to the third stratum and find them eight persons; similarly every stratum becomes theirs; it is divided among them, and what was before becomes void. (End). So some contemporaries took from the eighth form, and the explanation of its ruling is that al-Khassaf is of the view of overturning the division in a case like al-Subki's issue, and he did not reflect on the difference between the two forms. For in al-Subki's issue: waqf on his children then their children with the word 'thumma' between the two layers. And in al-Khassaf's issue: waqf on his child and his child's child with 'wa' not 'thumma'. So the beginning of al-Khassaf's issue necessitated the sharing of the upper stratum with the lower, and the beginning of al-Subki's issue necessitated no sharing. So the statement of overturning the division or not is based on this. The evidence for this is that al-Khassaf after establishing the overturning of the division as we mentioned said: If you say: Why is this statement the one acted upon according to you, and you abandoned his statement that whenever death occurs to any of them, his share is returned to his child and his child's child and his descendants forever as long as they reproduce? I say: Because some of them enter into the produce and their right in it is due by themselves not by their father, so we acted accordingly and divided the produce among them. (End). Thus it is understood that the cause of overturning is the inclusion of the child's child with the child at the beginning of the statement. So if its beginning does not include the child's child with the child but rather excludes him, how can it be said to overturn the division? If you say: You are right that al-Khassaf depicted it with 'wa', but he mentioned afterwards what gives the meaning of 'thumma', i.e., giving precedence to the upper stratum, so they became equal. I say: Yes, but that is exclusion after inclusion in the first, unlike the expression with 'thumma' from the beginning of the statement, for the second stratum did not enter with the first. So how can it be correct to use al-Khassaf's statement as evidence for al-Subki's issue, while al-Subki built the statement of overturning the division on that if the waqif mentions two conflicting conditions, we act on the first of them? He said: This is not abrogation such that we act on the later. If this is al-Subki's opinion regarding two conditions, then there is no argument about not relying on it. And if it is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him), it is problematic according to their statement: 'The condition of the waqif is like the text of the Lawgiver', for it requires acting on the later. And since al-Subki's statement was based on that, it is not valid to say it according to our madhhab; for our madhhab is to act on the later of the two. Imam al-Khassaf said: If he wrote at the beginning of the document after the waqf: 'It is not to be sold nor given as a gift', and wrote at its end: 'with the condition that so-and-so has that and the substitution for its price', then substitution is permissible for him. He said: Because the later abrogates the former. And if it were the opposite, its sale would be prohibited. (End). So the conclusion is that if the waqif makes a waqf on his children and his children's children, and on his grandchildren, and on his descendants, generation after generation and layer after layer, the upper layer blocks the lower. With the condition that whoever dies leaving a child, his share transfers to his child, and whoever dies without a child, his share transfers to those who are in his grade and of his layer; and with the condition that whoever dies before entering this waqf and before becoming entitled to any of its benefits, and leaves a child or a grandchild or lower, he is entitled to what his father would have been entitled to if he were alive. This form frequently occurs in Cairo, but some express it with 'thumma' between layers, and some with 'wa'. If it is with 'wa', the waqf is divided between the upper layer and the children of the deceased during the waqif's life before their entry; they get what would have been allocated to their fathers if they were alive with their brothers. So whoever among the waqif's children dies leaving a child, his share goes to his child, and whoever dies without a child, his share goes to his brothers. This continues until the extinction of the upper stratum, and this is al-Khassaf's issue in which he said overturn the division, where it was mentioned with 'wa', as you have known. And if it is mentioned with 'thumma', then whoever from the people of the first stratum dies leaving a child, his share transfers to his child and remains for him, and it is not overturned at all afterwards, even if the people of the first stratum become extinct. So if one of the two children of the waqif dies leaving a child and the other leaves ten, then half is for the child of the one who died having a child and the other half for the ten. When the two sons of the waqif die, the half continues for the one and the half for the ten. And if they are equal in the layer, his statement 'on condition that whoever dies having a child' is specific from the arrangement of layers, so arrangement is not observed in it. Then whoever has something, it transfers to his child, and so on until the end of the layers. Even if it is assumed that the waqif died leaving two sons, then...
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis of a waqf ruling concerning the division of usufruct among multiple generations of beneficiaries. The core issue is whether the division of proceeds must be overturned (naqd al-qisma) when the upper generation of beneficiaries dies out, depending on the wording used by the waqif (founder). The text contrasts two scenarios: one where the waqf is made using the conjunction 'wa' (and) between layers, and another using 'thumma' (then). It begins by describing a hypothetical: a waqf on ten children, with the condition that if one dies, his share goes to his own children. When all ten die, the division is overturned and the proceeds are divided among the grandchildren equally, ignoring the original condition. This is because the waqif's wording 'on his children and his children's children' includes the grandchildren simultaneously with the children, so when the children die out, the division resets to the next layer. The text then examines a ruling by the Hanafi jurist al-Khassaf, who held that if the waqf is worded with 'wa', the upper and lower layers share from the beginning, and when the upper layer dies, the division is overturned. In contrast, al-Subki (likely a Shafi'i scholar) is reported to have ruled that when two conditions conflict, the earlier one is followed, thus no overturning. The Hanafi position, supported by al-Khassaf, is that the later condition abrogates the earlier, analogous to the principle that later stipulations in a contract supersede earlier ones. The passage emphasizes that the difference in wording is crucial: 'wa' implies simultaneous inclusion, while 'thumma' implies sequential exclusion. Therefore, in cases where the waqif uses 'thumma', the children of a deceased beneficiary inherit his share without overturning the overall division, even after the entire upper stratum dies. The text concludes with practical applications in Cairo, noting that fatwas depend on the exact wording used in the waqf deed. This analysis exemplifies the Hanafi methodology of interpreting the waqif's intent through precise linguistic analysis and applying the principle that later conditions abrogate earlier ones in case of conflict.

Key Terms
نقض القسمة (naqd al-qisma): The act of overturning the initial division of waqf proceeds and redistributing them among the next generation of beneficiaries upon the extinction of the upper stratum.
بطن (batn): A generation or stratum of beneficiaries in a waqf, e.g., the first stratum (children), second stratum (grandchildren), etc.
شرط الواقف كنص الشارع (shart al-wāqif ka-nass al-shāriʿ): A legal maxim meaning that the condition stipulated by the founder of a waqf is treated with the same binding force as a legal text from the Lawgiver (Allah or the Prophet).
الآخر ناسخ للأول (al-akhir nasikh li'l-awwal): A sub-maxim meaning 'the later abrogates the earlier', used to resolve conflicts between successive conditions in a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in the text, if a waqf deed contains two conflicting conditions, which condition should be acted upon?
A) The earlier condition, because it was written first.
B) The later condition, because later stipulations abrogate earlier ones.
C) Neither; the waqf becomes invalid.
D) Both conditions are applied together wherever possible.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf jurisprudence, the later condition abrogates the earlier, but the ruling on overturning division (naqd al-qisma) depends critically on whether the generations are joined by 'wa' (simultaneous inclusion) or 'thumma' (sequential exclusion).

Foundation is Better than Confirmation: Applications in Divorce and Oaths
Application of qawida to divorce and oaths with rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-Ta'sis khayr min al-ta'kid' to determine the ruling in cases of repeated utterances in divorce and oaths, and identify the exceptions.

Core Idea
When a repeated utterance can be foundation or confirmation, foundation is preferred in court; diyanatan, intention may be accepted.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi legal maxim: 'al-Ta'sis khayr min al-ta'kid' (Foundation is better than confirmation). This maxim governs how we interpret repeated utterances in legal contexts like divorce and oaths. The source passage from *al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir* presents the rule and applies it to three scenarios, showing how the default is foundation unless exceptional circumstances apply. As a mufti, knowing when to apply the maxim and when to accept a claim of confirmation is essential for issuing correct fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the repeated utterance
Determine what was said and the legal context (divorce or oath).
The repetition may be in the same or different gatherings.
2
Apply the default: foundation
Assume each repetition creates a new legal effect (e.g., a new divorce or a new oath).
This is the default interpretation.
3
Consider the possibility of confirmation
Check if the speaker claims confirmation (i.e., intended the repetition to emphasize the first).
This claim is relevant only diyanatan.
4
Distinguish between qada and diyana
In court (qada), foundation is followed; in religious conscience (diyana), confirmation may be accepted.
Diyaatan acceptance requires truthfulness.
5
Check for exceptions
For oaths by Allah, the claim of confirmation is not accepted even diyanatan; for oaths by Hajj/Umrah, it is accepted.
This exception is from Imam Muhammad in al-Asl and al-Tajrid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه يدخل في هذه القاعدة قولهم : التأسيس خير من التأكيد. فإذا دار اللفظ بينهما تعين الحمل على التأسيس، ولذا قال أصحابنا : لو قال لزوجته؛ أنت طالق طالق طالق طلقت ثلاثا، فإن قال أردت به التأكيد صدق ديانة لا قضاء. ذكره الزيلعي في الكنايات. وفي الخلاصة : إذا حلف على أمر ألا يفعله ثم حلف في ذلك المجلس أو في مجلس آخر ألا يفعله أبدا ، ثم فعله ؛ إن نوى يمينًا مبتدأ أو التشديد أو لم ينو شيئًا فعليه كفَّارة يمينين، وإن نوى بالثاني الأول فعليه كفارة واحدة. وفي التجريد عن أبي حنيفة : إذا حلف بأيمان فعليه لكل يمين كفَّارة، والمجلس والمجالس فيه سواء، ولو قال عنيت بالثاني الأول لم يستقم ذلك في اليمين بالله تعالى. ولو حلف بحجة أو عمرة يستقيم. وفي الأصل أيضًا لو قال هو يهودي وهو نصراني إن فعل كذا، يمين
Note: Included in this rule is their statement: 'Foundation is better than confirmation.' So if the wording can be interpreted either way, it is determined to be foundation. For this reason, our companions said: If a man says to his wife: 'You are divorced, divorced, divorced,' she is divorced three times. If he says: 'I intended confirmation,' he is believed in religion (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an). Al-Zayla'i mentioned it in al-Kinayat. And in al-Khulasah: If one swears an oath not to do something, then swears in that same gathering or another gathering not to do it ever, then does it; if he intended a new oath or emphasis or did not intend anything, he owes two expiations. If he intended by the second the first, he owes one expiation. And in al-Tajrid from Abu Hanifah: If he swears multiple oaths, he owes an expiation for each oath, whether in one gathering or multiple gatherings. If he says: 'I meant by the second the first,' that is not valid in oaths by Allah Almighty. But if he swore by Hajj or 'Umrah, it is valid. And in al-Asl also: If he says: 'He is a Jew, he is a Christian if he does such and such,' an oath.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This is a 'tanbih' (note) on a qawida (legal maxim) included under a broader rule. The maxim is: 'al-Ta'sis khayr min al-ta'kid' (Foundation is better than confirmation). The principle: when a word or phrase could be understood either as establishing a new legal effect (foundation) or as merely confirming a previous one, the interpretation that gives a new effect is preferred. The author then gives three applications from Hanafi law. First, divorce: if a man says 'You are divorced, divorced, divorced' to his wife, the default interpretation is three divorces (each utterance is a new divorce). If he claims he intended confirmation (i.e., repeating for emphasis), he is believed in his private conscience (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an), because the outward apparent meaning is foundation. Second, oaths: if someone swears not to do something, then swears again (in same or different gathering) not to do it ever, then does it; if his intention was a new oath or emphasis, or he had no intention, he owes two expiations (one for each oath). But if he intended the second to refer back to the first (i.e., confirmation), he owes only one expiation. However, from Abu Hanifah in al-Tajrid: for oaths by Allah, saying 'I meant the second as confirmation of the first' is not accepted because each oath by Allah is independent. But if the oath is by Hajj or 'Umrah, it can be accepted. Third, in al-Asl: if someone says 'He is a Jew, he is a Christian if he does such and such,' this is considered an oath. The flow shows that the qawida governs interpretation in matters of intention, with nuances in different contexts.

Key Terms
التأسيس (al-ta'sis): Interpreting a repeated utterance as establishing a new legal effect (e.g., a new divorce or new oath)
التأكيد (al-ta'kid): Interpreting a repeated utterance as merely confirming the first (no new legal effect)
ديانة (diyanatan): In religious conscience (between the person and Allah); not enforceable in court
قضاء (qada'an): In court; based on outward apparent meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife: 'You are divorced, divorced, divorced.' He claims he intended confirmation. In court, how many divorces occur?
A) One divorce.
B) Three divorces.
C) No divorce because repetition is invalid.
D) Two divorces.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-Ta'sis khayr min al-ta'kid' ensures legal certainty by interpreting repeated utterances as creating new legal effects, with limited exceptions for certain oaths.

The Maxim: Establishing Over Confirming (التأسيس خير من التأكيد)
Explanation of the maxim 'التأسيس خير من التأكيد' with furu' from divorce and oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'التأسيس خير من التأكيد' to cases of repeated utterances in divorce and oaths, distinguishing between tasis and tawkid in qada' and diyana.

Core Idea
When a word can mean either new or confirm, the new meaning prevails in court.

This lesson introduces a powerful control rule (dabt) in Hanafi ifta: 'التأسيس خير من التأكيد' — establishing is better than confirming. When someone repeats a statement like divorce or an oath, the default is that each repetition creates a new legal effect, unless the speaker's intention to confirm is accepted between him and Allah. This principle guides many practical fatwa scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its scope
State the maxim and recognize that it applies to any utterance that could be interpreted as either establishing a new legal effect (tasis) or confirming a previous one (tawkid).
The maxim is a dabt, not a kulli qa'ida; it controls interpretation when repetition occurs.
2
Classify the utterance by context
Determine the legal context (divorce, oath, etc.) and whether the repetition is in the same session or separate sessions. This affects the ruling.
For oaths, the ruling also depends on the object of the oath (e.g., by Allah vs. by Hajj).
3
Apply the default rule of tasis in qada'
In a judicial (qada') setting, the default is to treat each repetition as establishing a new effect. Intention is only considered diyanatan (between the person and Allah).
This means the mufti rules based on the apparent meaning in court, but privately may accept the person's claim of tawkid.
4
Identify exceptions and special cases
For oaths by Allah, the claim of tawkid is not accepted even diyanatan, because the gravity of Allah's name prevents such nullification. For other oaths (e.g., by Hajj), tawkid may be accepted.
Also, when oaths are connected vs. separated, the number of expiations varies.
5
Work through the furuat from the passage
Map each example from the source (divorce triple, two oaths, multiple durations) to the maxim and note the ruling in qada' and diyana.
Practice tracing the reasoning from maxim to ruling for each case.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه
يدخل في هذه القاعدة قولهم : التأسيس خير من التأكيد. فإذا دار اللفظ بينهما تعين الحمل على التأسيس، ولذا قال أصحابنا : لو قال لزوجته؛ أنت طالق طالق طالق طلقت ثلاثا، فإن قال أردت به التأكيد صدق ديانة لا قضاء. ذكره الزيلعي في الكنايات. وفي الخلاصة : إذا حلف على أمر ألا يفعله ثم حلف في ذلك المجلس أو في مجلس آخر ألا يفعله أبدا ، ثم فعله ؛ إن نوى يمينًا مبتدأ أو التشديد أو لم ينو شيئًا فعليه كفَّارة يمينين، وإن نوى بالثاني الأول فعليه كفارة واحدة. وفي التجريد عن أبي حنيفة : إذا حلف بأيمان فعليه لكل يمين كفَّارة، والمجلس والمجالس فيه سواء، ولو قال عنيت بالثاني الأول لم يستقم ذلك في اليمين بالله تعالى. ولو حلف بحجة أو عمرة يستقيم. وفي الأصل أيضًا لو قال هو يهودي وهو نصراني إن فعل كذا، يمين واحدة، ولو قال هو يهودي إن فعل كذا هو نصراني إن فعل كذا، فهما يمينان. وفي النوازل: رجل قال لآخر : والله لا أكلمه يوما والله لا أكلمه شهرًا، والله لا أكلمه سنة . إن كلمه بعد ساعة فعليه ثلاثة أيمان وإن كلّمه بعد الغد فعليه يمينان، وإن كلمه بعد شهر فعليه يمين واحدة وإن كلمه بعد سنة فلا شيء عليه. انتهى ما في الخلاصة)
Admonition: Included in this maxim is their statement: 'Establishing is better than confirming.' So if the word circulates between them, it is determined to be establishing. Hence our companions said: If he said to his wife, 'You are divorced, divorced, divorced,' she is divorced three times. If he said, 'I intended confirmation,' he is believed in private (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an). Al-Zayla'i mentioned it in al-Kinayat. And in al-Khulasa: If he swore about something not to do it, then swore again in the same session or another session not to do it ever, then did it: if he intended a new oath or emphasis or did not intend anything, he owes two expiations; if he intended by the second the first, he owes one expiation. And in al-Tajrid from Abu Hanifa: If he swore multiple oaths, he owes expiation for each oath, and the session or sessions are the same, and if he said, 'I meant by the second the first,' that is not valid in an oath by Allah Almighty. But if he swore by Hajj or 'Umrah, it is valid. And in al-Asl also: If he said, 'He is a Jew, he is a Christian if he does such-and-such,' it is one oath; if he said, 'He is a Jew if he does such-and-such, he is a Christian if he does such-and-such,' then they are two oaths. And in al-Nawazil: A man said to another, 'By Allah, I will not speak to him for a day; by Allah, I will not speak to him for a month; by Allah, I will not speak to him for a year.' If he speaks to him after an hour, he owes three oaths; if after the next day, he owes two; if after a month, one; if after a year, nothing. End of what is in al-Khulasa.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the maxim 'التأسيس خير من التأكيد' (Establishing is better than confirming). The author begins by stating that when a word or phrase can be interpreted either as establishing a new legal effect or as confirming a previous one, the interpretation that establishes something new is preferred. This is a well-known principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. He then provides several furu' (applications) from the chapters of divorce and oaths. The first application: if a man says to his wife 'you are divorced, divorced, divorced,' she is divorced three times in court because the default is to treat each repetition as a new divorce (tasis). However, if the husband claims he intended only to confirm (tawkid), he is believed between him and Allah (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an), because the apparent meaning is tasis. The second application: from the book al-Khulasa regarding multiple oaths. If someone swears two oaths about the same matter in the same or different sessions, the ruling depends on his intention. If he intends a new oath or emphasis, or has no intention, two expiations are required. If he intends the second oath to be a confirmation of the first, only one expiation is due. However, according to Abu Hanifa in al-Tajrid, if the oaths are by Allah, one cannot nullify the second by claiming confirmation; such a claim is only valid for oaths by Hajj or 'Umrah. The third application: from al-Asl, if someone says 'He is a Jew, he is a Christian if he does such-and-such,' it is one oath because the two clauses are connected; but if he separates them with a pause, they become two oaths. The fourth application: from al-Nawazil, a man swears three different durations: a day, a month, a year. If he breaks the oath by speaking after an hour, he violates all three and owes three expiations; after a day, two; after a month, one; after a year, none. These examples demonstrate how the principle of tasis vs tawkid operates in practice.

Key Terms
تأسيس (ta'sīs): Establishing a new legal effect, such as a new divorce or new obligation
تأكيد (tawkīd): Confirmation of a previous statement without adding a new legal effect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle 'التأسيس خير من التأكيد', if a man says to his wife 'أنت طالق طالق طالق' and then claims he intended confirmation (تأكيد), what is the judicial (qada') ruling?
A) She is divorced three times.
B) She is divorced once because of his claim.
C) The judge must investigate his intention.
D) No divorce occurs until he clarifies.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, repetition in utterances like divorce and oaths is presumed to establish new effects (tasis) unless the speaker is believed diyanatan, and for oaths by Allah, tawkid is not accepted at all.

Al-Kharaj bi al-Daman: The Maxim of Benefit with Liability
Presentation of the hadith-based maxim with commentary and a scholarly question

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' to cases of return due to defect, identify its exception for pre-possession increases, and evaluate the reasoning behind the Hanafi ruling.

Core Idea
Earnings from an asset belong to the one who bears the risk of loss.

The maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' is a foundational principle in Hanafi mu'amalat. It originates from a prophetic hadith and governs who keeps the profits from an asset when it is returned due to a defect. The maxim is not absolute; there is a notable exception regarding increases before possession. This lesson unpacks the hadith, its application, and the subtlety of its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its source
Read the hadith from 'Aisha and note the context: a slave was bought, used, then returned due to a hidden defect. The Prophet ruled that the buyer keeps the earnings because he bore the risk.
The hadith is sahih according to the author.
2
Define the key terms 'kharaj' and 'daman'
Kharaj is any yield or profit from an asset (e.g., wages, rent, fruit, milk). Daman is liability for loss; the person who would bear the loss if the asset perishes is entitled to its profits.
These definitions are from Abu 'Ubayd and al-Fa'iq.
3
Understand the application in khiyar al-'ayb
The Hanafi rule: when a buyer returns a defective item, any non-integral increase (e.g., earnings from using it) belongs to the buyer and does not prevent return. The buyer keeps the profit even though he gets a full refund.
The increase is not part of the sold item; it is earned through liability.
4
Analyze the exception for pre-possession increases
The author raises a question: if kharaj is in exchange for daman, then before possession (qabd), the seller bears the risk, so pre-possession increases should belong to the seller. The answer is that before possession, the reasoning is ownership (milk), not liability. After possession, both ownership and liability apply.
This nuance prevents the maxim from applying to pre-delivery profits.
5
Apply the maxim to a contemporary scenario
Consider a buyer who purchases a car, uses it for delivery services for a week, then finds a defect and returns it. According to the maxim, the buyer keeps the delivery earnings. The seller must refund the full price.
Ensure the car was in the buyer's possession and risk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة العاشرة : الخراج بالضمان
هو حديث صحيح رواه أحمد وأبو داود والترمذي والنسائي وابن ماجه وابن حبان من حديث عائشة رضي الله عنها، وفي بعض طرقه ذكر السبب؛ وهو أن رجلاً ابتاع عبدا فأقام عنده ما شاء الله أن يقيم، ثم وجد به عيبا فخاصمه إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فرده عليه فقال الرجل: يا رسول الله قد استعمل غلامي. فقال: «الخراج بالضمان».
قال أبو عبيد : الخراج في هذا الحديث غلة العبد ؛ يشتريه الرجل فيستعمله زمانًا ثم يعثر منه على عيب دلسه البائع فيرده ويأخذ جميع الثمن ويفوز بغلته كلها لأنه كان في ضمانه، ولو هلك هلك من ماله . (انتهى).
وفي الفائق : كل ما خرج من شيء فهو خراجه ؛ فخراج الشجر ثمره، وخراج الحيوان دره ونسله (انتهى).
وذكر فخر الإسلام في أصوله أن هذا الحديث من جوامع الكلم، لا يجوز نقله بالمعنى. وقال أصحابنا في باب خيار العيب إن الزيادة غير المتولدة من الأصل لا تمنع الردّ بالعيب كالكسب والغلة وتسلم المشتري ولا يضر حصولها له مجانا لأنها لم تكن جزءًا من المبيع فلم يملكها بالثمن، وإنما ملكها بالضمان وبمثله يطيب الربح للحديث. وهنا سؤالان لم أرهما لأصحابنا :
أحدهما لو كان الخراج في مقابلة الضمان لكانت الزوائد قبل القبض للبائع، تم العقد أو انفسخ، لكونه من ضمانه ولا قائل به وأجيب بأن الخراج يعلل قبل القبض بالملك وبعده به وبالضمان معًا. واقتصر في الحديث على التعليل بالضمان لأنه أظهر عند البائع وأقطع لطلبه واستبعاده أن الخراج للمشتري.
The Tenth Maxim: Al-Kharaj bi al-Daman (Benefit is with Liability). It is an authentic hadith narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, and Ibn Hibban from the hadith of 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her. In some of its chains, the reason is mentioned: a man bought a slave, kept him as long as Allah willed, then found a defect and complained to the Prophet (peace be upon him), so he returned him. The man said, 'O Messenger of Allah, my slave has worked.' He said: 'Al-Kharaj bi al-Daman.' Abu 'Ubayd said: 'Kharaj in this hadith means the earnings of the slave; the man buys it, uses it for a time, then discovers a defect that the seller concealed, so he returns him and takes all the price, and gains all its earnings because it was in his liability; had it perished, it would have perished from his wealth.' (End). And in al-Fa'iq: 'Everything that comes out of something is its kharaj; so the kharaj of a tree is its fruit, and the kharaj of an animal is its milk and offspring.' (End). And Fakhr al-Islam mentioned in his Usul that this hadith is from the comprehensive statements; it is not permissible to transmit it by meaning. And our companions said in the chapter of option of defect that an increase not derived from the original does not prevent return due to defect, such as earnings and produce; the buyer retains them, and their free acquisition does not harm him, because they were not part of the sold item, so he did not own them by the price; rather, he owned them by liability, and similarly profit is permissible due to the hadith. And here are two questions that I have not seen from our companions: One of them: If al-kharaj is in exchange for al-daman, then the increases before possession would belong to the seller, whether the contract was completed or rescinded, because they were under his liability, and no one says that. And it was answered that al-kharaj is reasoned before possession by ownership, and after it by ownership and liability together. The hadith limited the reasoning to liability because it is more apparent to the seller and cuts off his claim and his surprise that the kharaj is for the buyer.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth maxim of the book: 'الخراج بالضمان' (Benefit is with Liability). It is a prophetic hadith reported from 'Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her). The author first establishes its authenticity and cites the context: a man bought a slave, used his services, then discovered a hidden defect and returned the slave. The Prophet ruled that the buyer keeps the earnings (kharaj) because he bore the risk (daman) during the period of possession; if the slave had died, the loss would have been his. Abu 'Ubayd explains that 'kharaj' here specifically means the earnings of the slave. The author then quotes al-Fa'iq to generalize the meaning: 'kharaj' applies to any yield or produce. Fakhr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi) notes the hadith's comprehensiveness and that it cannot be transmitted by meaning, i.e., the exact wording is legally binding. The Hanafi scholars then apply this maxim to the chapter of option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb): any increase that is not physically generated from the original item (e.g., earnings, rent) does not prevent the buyer from returning the defective item; the buyer keeps the increase even though he gets a full refund, because the increase was earned through liability (daman), not through the price. This is the basis for the permissibility of profit in sales generally. The author then raises a question he claims not to have seen addressed by earlier Hanafis: if the maxim states that kharaj is in exchange for daman, then before possession (qabd), the increase should belong to the seller because he bears the risk before transfer. But no Hanafi scholar says that; they assign pre-possession increases to the seller by virtue of ownership, not liability. The answer given is that before possession, the reasoning is ownership (milk); after possession, it is ownership and liability together. The hadith specifically mentions liability because it is more obvious to the seller and prevents him from disputing that the buyer should keep the profit.

Key Terms
الخراج (al-kharāj): The yield or earnings from an asset, especially the wages of a slave
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability for loss or damage; the obligation to compensate if the asset is destroyed
القبض (al-qabḍ): Physical possession of the purchased item

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the lesson, what is the reason that a buyer who returns a defective slave keeps the slave's earnings?
A) The buyer owned the slave during the period.
B) The buyer bore the risk of loss during that period.
C) The seller was negligent in disclosing the defect.
D) The earnings were part of the sale price.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' means that profit follows liability, not necessarily ownership, and it only applies after the buyer takes possession of the asset.

The Maxim 'al-Su'al Mu'id fi al-Jawab' – The Question is Reproduced in the Answer
Explaining the rule that an answer incorporates the terms of the question, with applications in oaths and divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-su'al mu'id fi al-jawab' to determine the legal effect of ambiguous responses (naam/bala) in oath and divorce cases, and distinguish between affirmative and negative question contexts.

Core Idea
The answer repeats the question — 'naam' or 'bala' are interpreted by the question's structure, not by ordinary meaning.

Have you ever wondered if a simple 'yes' always means agreement in Islamic law? In Hanafi fiqh, the answer is no. The maxim 'al-su'al mu'id fi al-jawab' teaches that the answer reproduces the question's wording and context. This lesson unpacks this crucial rule through practical examples from oaths and divorce, showing how a mufti must analyze the entire question to interpret the response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its core meaning
The maxim 'al-su'al mu'id fi al-jawab' means the answer incorporates the question's terms. So 'naam' or 'bala' are not simple affirmations; they confirm the whole question.
2
Analyze the first furu': compound conditional oath
When Zaid says 'naam' to a compound statement (wife divorced, slave free, Hajj if enter house), the answer confirms all parts, binding Zaid to all three. Contrast: 'I permit that' is not an oath because it does not repeat the conditional.
Al-Bazzazi distinguishes between 'naam' and other responses like 'ajaztu'.
3
Analyze the divorce furuat: question type matters
For 'Am I divorced?' 'naam' confirms divorce. For 'Divorce me!' 'naam' is ambiguous and requires intention. For negative question 'Did you not divorce?', 'bala' affirms divorce, while 'naam' negates it.
The key is whether the question is declarative or imperative, positive or negative.
4
Summarize the practical application for muftis
When issuing fatwas, always examine the wording of the question to interpret the answer. This maxim prevents misinterpretation of simple affirmations.
The maxim is a dabt (restrictive rule) that complements the broader qa'ida 'al-ibra bi al-siyagh la bi al-ma'ani'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الحادية عشرة : السؤال معاد في الجواب
قال البزازي في فتاواه من أواخر الوكالة وعن الثاني لو قال: امرأة زيد طالق وعبده حرّ وعليه المشي إلى بيت الله تعالى الحرام إن دخل هذه الدار؟ فقال زيد: نعم. كان زيد حالفًا بكله لأن الجواب يتضمن إعادة ما في السؤال، ولو قال أجزت ذلك ولم يقل نعم فهو لم يحلف على شيء، ولو قال أجزت ذلك على إن دخلت الدار وألزمته نفسي إن دخلت لزم، وإن دخل قبل الإجازة لا يقع شيء
إلى آخره.
وفيها من كتاب الطلاق قالت له أنا طالق ؟ فقال : نعم، تطلق. ولو قالت طلقني فقال: نعم ،لا، وإن نوى قيل له ألست طلقت امرأتك؟ قال: بلى. طلقت لأنه جواب الاستفهام بالإثبات ولو قال نعم لا، لأنه جواب الاستفهام بالنفي. كأنه قال نعم ما طلقت. (انتهى).
The Eleventh Rule: The Question is Reproduced in the Answer.
Al-Bazzazi said in his Fatawa, from the end of the chapter of Agency (al-Wakala): 'From the second: If someone said: "Zaid's wife is divorced, his slave free, and he must walk to the Sacred House of Allah if he enters this house?" And Zaid said: "Yes." Then Zaid is bound by all three, because the answer entails repeating what is in the question. If he said: "I permit that" and did not say "Yes," then he has not sworn to anything. If he said: "I permit that on condition that if he enters the house, I will bind myself," and he enters, he is bound, but if he enters before the permission, nothing occurs, etc.'
And in the same book, from the chapter of Divorce (Talaq): 'She said to him: "Am I divorced?" He said: "Yes." She is divorced. If she said: "Divorce me," and he said: "Yes," no [divorce occurs] unless he intended. If he was asked: "Did you not divorce your wife?" He said: "Bala (yes)." She is divorced, because it is the answer to an interrogative affirmative. If he said: "Yes," no, because it is the answer to an interrogative negative. As if he said: "Yes, I did not divorce." (End).
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the rule 'al-su'al mu'id fi al-jawab' (the question is reproduced in the answer), which is crucial for interpreting simple responses like 'naam' (yes) and 'bala' (yes, in response to a negative question) in legal contexts such as oaths and divorce. Al-Bazzazi's Fatawa provides several applications. First, if a person is asked a compound conditional statement ('Zaid's wife is divorced, his slave free, and he must go for Hajj if he enters this house') and simply replies 'naam', he confirms the entire statement and is bound by all parts. The answer effectively repeats the question. If he says 'I permit that' instead of 'naam', no oath is made because the response is not a direct affirmation of the condition. Second, in divorce, if a wife asks 'Am I divorced?' and the husband says 'naam', she is divorced because the answer repeats the divorce declaration. If she says 'Divorce me!' and he says 'naam', no divorce occurs unless he had the intention to divorce, because 'naam' to a command is ambiguous. Third, when a man is asked 'Did you not divorce your wife?' (a negative question), if he says 'bala', it confirms the affirmative ('Yes, I did divorce'), so divorce occurs. If he says 'naam', it is interpreted as repeating the negative question ('Yes, I did not divorce'), so no divorce. This rule helps muftis and judges determine the legal effect of ambiguous answers by looking at the structure of the question.

Key Terms
السؤال معاد في الجواب (al-su'al mu'id fi al-jawab): A legal maxim meaning that the answer adopts the wording and context of the question, so ambiguous words like 'naam' or 'bala' are interpreted according to the question's phrasing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man is asked: 'Did you not divorce your wife?' and he replies 'Bala' (yes), what is the ruling according to Hanafis?
A) No divorce
B) Divorce occurs
C) It depends on his intention
D) The answer is invalid

Key Takeaway
The ruling for 'naam' and 'bala' depends on the structure of the question: the answer repeats the question, so it can either affirm or deny accordingly.

Scope of the Maxim 'Al-Kharaj bi al-Daman'
Second point on the maxim al-kharaj bi al-daman: scope and applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' to determine the disposition of profits in cases of surety, corrupt sale, and usurpation, and identify the exception regarding wala' and 'aql.

Core Idea
The maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' applies only to ownership liability; a usurper does not own benefits of usurpation.

This lesson examines the second point under the well-known Hanafi maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' (profits follow liability). The passage clarifies that the maxim is not universal: it governs cases where the liability is one of ownership, not mere possession. The usurper (ghasib), despite his stricter liability, does not become owner of the item's benefits. The discussion then applies the maxim to surety (kafalah) and corrupt sale (bay' fasid), and concludes with an exception to the usual rule connecting wala' and 'aql.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its apparent implication
Begin by articulating the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' – profits follow liability. At first glance, this could imply that anyone bearing liability for an item is entitled to its profits.
2
Identify the limitation: ownership vs. possession liability
The passage argues that the maxim is limited to cases of liability of ownership (daman al-milk). The usurper has liability of possession (daman al-yad) but not ownership, so he does not own the benefits.
This distinction is the key to understanding the Hanafi position on usurpers' profits.
3
Apply to the surety case (kafalah)
When a principal pays the debt to the surety before discharge, and the surety profits from the money, the profit is lawful (tayyib) for the surety according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. This is because the surety's liability is akin to ownership in this context.
Abu Hanifa differed: one narration says return it, another says give in charity.
4
Apply to the corrupt sale (bay' fasid)
After rescission of a corrupt sale, the profit belongs to the seller, not the buyer, because the seller's liability is of ownership, while the buyer's possession is not ownership-based.
This illustrates the maxim applied in sale transactions.
5
Identify the istithna' (exception)
The passage notes an exception: if a woman frees a male slave, the wala' (patronage) goes to her son, but the 'aql (blood money) for wrongful homicide falls on her agnates, not the son. This departs from the usual correlation between wala' and 'aql.
This exception shows that the maxim is not absolute; other principles may override.

الثاني : لو كانت الغلة بالضمان لزم أن تكون الزوائد للغاصب؛ لأن ضمانه أشد من ضمان غيره. وبهذا احتج لأبي حنيفة في قوله إن الغاصب لا يضمن منافع الغصب. وأجيب بأنه قضى بدلك في ضمان الملك وجعل الخراج لمن هو مالكه إذا تلف تلف على ملكه وهو المشتري. والغاصب لا يملك المغصوب، وبأن الخراج هو المنافع جعلها لمن عليه الضمان ولا خلاف أن الغاصب لا يملك المغصوب، بل إذا أتلفها فالخلاف في ضمانها عليه فلا يتناول موضع الخلاف، ذكره الأسيوطي. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد فيما إذا دفع الأصيل الدين إلى الكفيل قبل الأداء فربح الكفيل فيه وكان مما يتعيَّن أن الربح يطيب له، واستدل لهما في فتح القدير بالحديث؛ فقال الإمام يردَّه على الأصيل في رواية، ويتصدق به في رواية. وقالوا في البيع الفاسد إذا فسخ فإنه يطيب للبائع ما ربح لا للمشتري .
·
والحاصل أن الخبث إن كان لعدم الملك فإن الربح لا يطيب كما إذا ربح في المغصوب والأمانة، ولا فرق بين المتعيّن ،وغيره وإن كان لفساد الملك طاب فيما لا يتعين لا فيما يتعيَّن ذكره الزيلعي في باب البيع الفاسد قال الأسيوطي: خرجت عن هذا الأصل مسألة وهي ما لو أعتقت المرأة عبدا فإن ولاءه يكون لابنها، ولو جنى جناية خطأ فالعقل على عصبتها دونه. وقد يجيء مثله في بعض العصابات يعقل ولا يرث . (انتهى).
وأما منقول مشايخنا فيها فلم أره .
The second point: If the yield (ghalla) were contingent upon liability (daman), then the accretions (zawa'id) would necessarily belong to the usurper (ghasib), because his liability is more severe than the liability of others. And with this, argument was made for Abu Hanifa in his statement that the usurper does not guarantee the benefits of usurpation. And it was answered that he (Abu Hanifa) ruled this in the case of liability of ownership and made the fruits (kharaj) for the one who is its owner when it perishes, it perishes upon his ownership, and that is the buyer. And the usurper does not own the usurped item, and because the fruits are the benefits, they were made for the one upon whom is the liability, and there is no disagreement that the usurper does not own the usurped item; rather, if he destroys them, the disagreement is about his liability for them, so it does not include the point of disagreement. Al-Asyuti mentioned this. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said, regarding the case when the principal (asil) pays the debt to the surety (kafil) before payment, and the surety profits from it, and it is something that is determined (muta'ayyan), that the profit is lawful (tayyib) for him. And in Fath al-Qadir, a hadith was cited as evidence for them; the Imam (Abu Hanifa) said: He should return it to the principal in one narration, and give charity with it in another narration. And they said regarding a corrupt sale (bay' fasid) when it is rescinded, the profit is lawful for the seller, not for the buyer.
And the conclusion is that if the impurity (khubth) is due to lack of ownership, then the profit is not lawful, as when one profits from a usurped item or a trust (amanah), and there is no difference between what is determined (muta'ayyan) and what is not. And if it is due to the corruption of ownership (fasad al-milk), it is lawful in what is not determined, but not in what is determined. Al-Zayla'i mentioned this in the chapter of corrupt sale. Al-Asyuti said: There is an issue that departs from this principle: if a woman frees a male slave, then his patronage (wala') belongs to her son, and if he commits a wrongful homicide, the blood money ('aql) is upon her agnates ('asabat) without him (the son). And similar can occur among some agnates who pay blood money but do not inherit. (End).
And as for what our scholars transmitted regarding this, I have not seen it.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage discusses the second point concerning the well-known Hanafi maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' (profits follow liability). The author begins by stating that if this maxim applied universally, a usurper would own all accretions since his liability is even stricter than that of a rightful owner. However, this is rebutted: Abu Hanifa's position is that the usurper does not guarantee the benefits of usurpation (manafi' al-ghasb). The reasoning is that the maxim applies to cases of ownership liability, not to usurpation where the usurper does not own the item. The profit from a usurped or entrusted item remains unlawful (khubth) due to lack of ownership. The text then presents applications: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule on profit from a surety's use of payment before discharge, with a difference of narration from Abu Hanifa. In a corrupt sale, profit after rescission goes to the seller, not the buyer. Finally, a departure from the principle is noted: if a woman frees a slave, the wala' goes to her son, but liability for blood money falls on her agnates, not the son—an exception to the usual rule that wala' and 'aql go together.

Key Terms
الغلة (al-ghalla): The yield, profit, or benefit generated from an asset, such as rent or produce.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability for loss or damage; in this context, the obligation to compensate for the item.
الغاصب (al-ghasib): A person who takes and holds property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The seller

D) Neither; it is charity

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' is restricted to ownership liability; profits from usurped or trust items remain unlawful.
