Maxim: Special Guardianship is Stronger than General Guardianship
A maxim on guardianship hierarchy with applications and a defining note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'special guardianship is stronger than general guardianship' to determine who has authority in marriage and qisas matters, and classify guardians into types based on their authority scope.

Core Idea
Special guardianship always overrides general guardianship; the judge steps in only when no specific guardian exists.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi legal maxim that establishes a clear hierarchy: a specific guardian (like a father or grandfather) holds stronger authority than a general guardian (the judge or imam). We'll see its application in marriage and qisas, and learn a definition (dabit) that categorizes guardians by their domain—property, marriage, or both. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for issuing correct fatwas on guardianship.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its meaning
Read the Arabic maxim and its translation. Understand that 'special guardianship' refers to a specific relationship (e.g., father), while 'general guardianship' is the judge's authority over those without a specific guardian.
The maxim is a sughra (minor) qawida, applicable in specific fiqh chapters.
2
Identify the scope and the first furu' (marriage)
The text states the judge cannot marry a minor orphan if a specific guardian exists. Consider why: the specific guardian's right is stronger.
This furu' is from the Nikah chapter.
3
Analyze the furu' in qisas
The special guardian can choose qisas, settlement, or forgiveness. The imam (general guardian) cannot forgive. Note the exception: the father of an incompetent (ma'tuh) can demand qisas or settle but not forgive.
The judge and executor can only settle (accept diyah).
4
Apply the dabit (definition) of guardian types
Classify guardians: (a) over property and marriage: father and grandfather; (b) over marriage only: other asaba, mother, relatives; (c) over property only: foreign executor. Note the hierarchy: property then marriage.
This dabit helps determine who has authority in a given situation.
5
Consider the istithna' (exception) to the maxim
The judge marries the minor when no specific guardian exists. This is not a violation of the maxim but an application of the principle 'harm must be removed' (الضرر يزال).
The exception confirms the rule: the judge's authority is residual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السادسة عشرة : الولاية الخاصة أقوى من الولاية العامة ولهذا قالوا إن القاضي لا يزوّج اليتيم واليتيمة إلا عند عدم ولي لهما في النكاح أما أن اسم عمله من محرم أو معتقاً. وللولي الخاص استيفاء القصاص والصلح والعفو ولو ذا أو رحم مجانا والإمام لا يملك العفو ولا يعارضه ما قال في الكنز : ولأب المعتوه القود والصلح لا العفو بقتل وليه لأنه فيما إذا قتل ولي المعتوه كابنه. قال في الكنز : والقاضي كالأب والوصي يصالح فقط أي فلا يقتل ولا يعفو . ضابط : الولي قد يكون وليا في المال والنكاح وهو الأب والجد، وقد يكون وليا في النكاح فقط وهو سائر العصبات والأم وذوو الأرحام، وقد يكون في المال فقط وهو الوصي الأجنبي. وظاهر كلام المشايخ أنها مراتب: المال ثم النكاح.
The sixteenth maxim: Special guardianship is stronger than general guardianship. For this reason they said that the judge does not marry a minor orphan boy or girl except when there is no guardian for them in marriage, but if the guardian is a specific one, whether a mahram or a freed slave. And the special guardian has the right to exact qisas, settle, and forgive, even if it is a relative for free, and the imam does not have the power to forgive, and what is said in al-Kanz: 'The father of an idiot has the right to qisas and settlement but not forgiveness for killing his ward, because when someone kills the ward like his son.' It is said in al-Kanz: 'The judge is like the father and the executor, he may only settle, meaning he does not execute qisas nor forgive.' Definition: The guardian may be a guardian over property and marriage, which is the father and grandfather, and may be a guardian over marriage only, which is the other asaba and the mother and relatives, and may be a guardian over property only, which is the foreign executor. And the apparent statement of the scholars is that these are levels: property then marriage.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This maxim states that a specific guardian (like a father) has stronger authority than a general guardian (like a judge). The first application: a judge cannot marry a minor orphan except when there is no specific guardian. The second: the specific guardian has the right to exact qisas, settle for diyah, or forgive, while the imam (ruler) does not have the right to forgive. The author then quotes from al-Kanz: the father of an incompetent person (ma'tuh) can demand qisas or settle, but cannot forgive the murderer of his child. The judge and the executor can only settle (i.e., accept diyah). Then a definition (dabit) of types of guardians: some have authority over both property and marriage (father, grandfather), some only over marriage (other asaba, mother, etc.), some only over property (foreign executor). The scholars indicate a hierarchy: property first, then marriage.

Key Terms
ولاية خاصة (wilaya khassa): Guardianship based on a specific relationship like fatherhood or grandfatherhood.
ولاية عامة (wilaya aamma): Guardianship held by a ruler or judge over those without a specific guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, what does a special guardian have that an imam does not?
A) Right to marry a minor
B) Right to forgive in qisas
C) Right to manage property
D) Right to execute a will

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the authority of a specific guardian (e.g., father) always takes precedence over the judge; the judge only acts in the absence of any specific guardian.

Furuat of the Maxim: Harmful Intent Does Not Invalidate Legally Complete Acts
Listing eight furuat of a prior maxim with a linguistic parallel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that actions performed with harmful intent remain valid to eight specific cases across different fiqh chapters and explain the common legal principle.

Core Idea
Harmful intent does not invalidate a legally complete act.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair presents eight furuat (applications) of a central Hanafi legal maxim. The maxim holds that if an action meets all its legal conditions and pillars, the actor's subjective intent to cause harm does not render it invalid. These cases span marriage, murder, inheritance, fasting, and zakat. Al-Suyuti even draws a parallel to Arabic grammar, where a subject can be described after fulfilling its object. We will work through each case to extract and apply this principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implicit maxim
The passage does not state the maxim explicitly, but the pattern across all eight cases is that harmful intent does not affect validity. Formulate the maxim: 'An action that fulfills its legal conditions remains valid even if performed with the intent to cause harm.'
This is a sub-maxim (sughra) of the broader Hanafi principle that legal effects follow objective fulfillment of conditions, not subjective intent in muamalat.
2
List and categorize the eight cases
Write down each case and identify the harmful intent and the legal outcome. Group them by fiqh chapter: talaq (case 1), murder (cases 1-3), marriage (cases 4-5), prayer (case 6), zakat (case 7), fasting (case 8).
Notice that harmful intent is absent from the legal consideration; the ruling is based on external conditions.
3
Extract the common principle
For each case, ask: What condition made the act valid despite the bad intent? For example, in case 1, the triple talaq is valid because the husband is of sound mind and the divorce is pronounced; the intent to deprive inheritance does not matter. The principle: validity is determined by objective fulfillment of shurut and arkan, not niyyah.
This distinguishes muamalat from ibadat, where niyyah is crucial for validity (e.g., wudu).
4
Apply the maxim to a new case
Imagine a person sells his house at a fair price just to prevent his neighbor from buying it. Using the maxim, the sale is valid because all conditions are met; the malicious intent is irrelevant.
This helps internalize the maxim and prepare for fatwa application.
5
Note the Arabic grammar parallel
Al-Suyuti's analogy: in Arabic, a subject (fa'il) can be described after its object is fulfilled; if described before, it is not allowed. Similarly, in legal acts, intent (which is like the description) can be examined only after the act's conditions are complete. Intent does not precede or invalidate the act.
Linguistic parallels are common in Hanafi usul to illustrate legal principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن فروعها لو طلقها ثلاثا بلا رضاها قاصدًا حرمانها من الإرث في مرض موته فإنها ترثه وخرجت عنها مسائل : الأولى : لو قتلت أم الولد سيدها عتقت ولا تحرم. الثانية : لو قتل المدبر سيده عتق، ولكن يسعى في جميع قيمته، لأنه لا وصية له. الثالثة : لو قتل صاحب الدين المديون حل دينه . الرابعة : أمسك زوجته مُسيئًا عشرتها لأجل إرثها ورثها. الخامسة : أمسكها كذلك لأجل الخلع نفذ. السادسة : شربت دواء فحاضت لم تقض الصلوات . السابعة باع مال الزكاة قبل الحول فرارًا عنها، صح ولم تجب. الثامنة : شرب شيئًا ليمرض قبل الفجر فأصبح مريضًا جاز له الفطر . قال السيوطي رحمه الله : رأيت لهذه القاعدة نظيرا في العربية، وهو الفاعل يجوز أن ينعت بعد استيفاء معموله فإن نعت قبيله امتنع أصله . (اهـ).
And among its furuat: if he divorced her three times without her consent intending to deprive her of inheritance during his terminal illness, then she inherits from him. And derived from it are issues: First: if a umm walad kills her master, she is freed and not deprived of inheritance. Second: if a mudabbar kills his master, he is freed but must work to pay his full value because there is no bequest for him. Third: if the creditor kills the debtor, his debt becomes due. Fourth: if he keeps his wife treating her badly for the sake of her inheritance, she inherits. Fifth: if he keeps her likewise for the sake of khul', it is valid. Sixth: if she drinks medicine and menstruates, she does not make up the prayers. Seventh: if he sells the zakat property before the year to escape it, it is valid and zakat is not due. Eighth: if he drinks something to become ill before dawn and becomes ill, it is permissible for him to break the fast. Al-Suyuti (may Allah have mercy on him) said: I saw for this maxim a parallel in Arabic grammar: the subject can be described after fulfilling its object, but if described before that, it is not allowed. (end).
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents applications (furuat) of a maxim that deals with actions performed with the intention of causing harm or achieving a prohibited goal, yet the actions themselves remain legally valid. The maxim itself is not explicitly stated here, but the pattern is clear: the legal effect hinges on the objective fulfillment of conditions, not on subjective intent to harm. The eight examples cover various chapters of fiqh: talaq, murder, marriage (khul'), prayer, zakat, and fasting. Each case shows that the act is valid despite the bad intention, because the necessary legal prerequisites were met. Al-Suyuti adds a linguistic parallel from Arabic grammar to illustrate the principle.

Key Terms
فروع (furu'): Legal applications or derived cases from a general maxim.
أم الولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death (tadbir).
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in return for a payment or waiver of rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a mudabbar who kills his master, what is the ruling according to the passage?
A) He is not freed and remains a slave.
B) He is freed and does not have to pay any compensation.
C) He is freed but must work to pay his full value.
D) He is freed but loses his right to inheritance.

Key Takeaway
The eight furuat demonstrate that harmful intent does not invalidate legally complete actions in muamalat; the maxim is a cornerstone for distinguishing between objective conditions and subjective motives in Hanafi fiqh.

Principle of Considering the Mukallaf's Belief vs Reality
Summarises the principle of considering belief versus reality with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-i'tibar lima zannah al-mukallaf' to classify new cases according to whether the mukallaf's belief or objective reality determines the ruling, and identify practical exceptions.

Core Idea
Belief governs in worship; reality governs in contracts and penalties.

When a person acts based on a mistaken assumption, does Islamic law honour that assumption or the objective facts? The answer depends on the legal domain. This lesson unpacks a concise Hanafi maxim that draws the boundary: in acts of worship, the mukallaf's diligent assumption is often respected (e.g., no penalty for a mistaken fast), while in marriage, debts, and hudud, objective reality overrides. A mufti must grasp this distinction to issue correct fatwas on cases of error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its two branches
The text begins by stating that in some matters, consideration is given to the mukallaf's assumption (zann), not to reality; in others, consideration is given to reality. Parse the Arabic phrasing to see the contrast.
The maxim is a sughra (minor) qawida, applicable across fiqh chapters.
2
Classify the examples by domain
The text lists cases from salat, nikah, hudud, siyam, hajj, debt, and divorce. For each, determine whether the ruling rests on the mukallaf's belief or on objective fact.
Note that in salat, reality determines (repeat); in siyam, belief mitigates (no kaffarah) but qada remains.
3
Apply the maxim to a new case
Given a scenario not in the text (e.g., a person makes tayammum thinking water is unavailable, then finds water), deduce the ruling using the maxim.
Use analogical reasoning: if the domain is ibadat, belief usually governs; if mu'amalat/hudud, reality governs.
4
Recognise exceptions and nuances
The text includes exceptions: even in hudud, a blind man who calls out is saved from hadd. In marriage, reality always governs. Understand the rationale behind the exceptions.
Exceptions often involve verification capacity or preservation of rights.
5
Formulate a fatwa summary
For a given case, produce a structured fatwa: state the case, cite the qawida, apply it, and note any relevant istithna.
This step integrates the maxim into ifta practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاصة سابقا على ما إذا لم يصل، أما إذا صلَّى فإنه يُعيد. ففي هذه المسائل الاعتبار لما ظنه المكلف لا لما في نفس الأمر، وعلى عكسه الاعتبار لما في نفس الأمر؛ فلو صلى وعنده أن الثوب طاهر أو أن الوقت قد دخل أو أنه متوضىء فبان خلافه أعاد. وينبغي أنه لو تزوج امرأة وعنده أنها غير محل فتبين أنها محل أو عكسه. أن يكون الاعتبار لما في نفس الأمر. وقالوا في الحدود: لو وطىء امرأة وجدها على فراشه ظانًا أنها امرأته فإنه يحدّ ولو كان أعمى. إلا إذا ناداها فأجابته. ولو أقر بطلاق زوجته ظانًا الوقوع بإفتاء المفتي فتبيَّن عدمه لم يقع كما في القنية. ولو أكل ظنه ليلاً فبانَ أنه بعد الطلوع قضى بلا تكفير ولو ظن الغروب فأكل ثم تبيَّن بقاء النهار قضى. وقالوا : لو رأوا سوادًا فظنوه عدوا فصلوا صلاة الخوف فبانَ خلافه لم تصح لأن الشرط حضور العدو وقالوا لو استناب المريض في حج ظانًا أنه لا يعيش ثم صح أداه بنفسه، ولو ظن أن عليه دينا فبان خلافه رجع بما أدّى، ولو خاطب امرأته بالطلاق ظانًا أنها أجنبية فبانَ أنها زوجته طلقت وكذا الفرض العتاق
The summary earlier depends on whether he did not pray, but if he prayed then he repeats. So in these matters, consideration is given to what the mukallaf assumed, not to what is in reality. And contrary to that, consideration is given to what is in reality: if he prayed thinking the garment was pure or that the time had entered or that he had wudu, and it became clear otherwise, he repeats. And it should be that if he married a woman thinking she was not permissible, and it becomes clear she is permissible, or vice versa, consideration is given to what is in reality. And they said regarding hudud: if a person has intercourse with a woman he finds on his bed thinking she is his wife, he is subject to hadd even if he is blind, unless he called her and she answered. And if he confessed to divorcing his wife thinking it occurred based on a mufti's fatwa, and it becomes clear it did not occur, the divorce does not occur as in al-Quniyah. And if he ate thinking it was night and it became clear it was after dawn, he makes qada without expiation; and if he thought sunset occurred and ate and then it became clear the day remained, he makes qada. And they said: if they saw darkness and thought it was enemies and prayed salat al-khawf, and it became clear otherwise, the prayer is invalid because the condition is the presence of the enemy. And they said: if a sick person appointed someone for Hajj thinking he would not live, then he recovered, he performs it himself. And if he thought he owed a debt and it became clear otherwise, he recovers what he paid. And if he addressed his wife with divorce thinking she was a stranger, and it became clear she is his wife, she is divorced, and likewise in the case of emancipation (al-fard al-'itaq?).
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings illustrating the principle of considering the mukallaf's belief (zann) versus objective reality. In acts of worship like prayer, if one acts on a mistaken assumption (e.g., thinking the time had entered), the act is invalid and must be repeated—belief governs. In matters of marriage and hudud, however, objective reality prevails: if one marries a permissible person thinking she is forbidden, the marriage is valid; if one commits zina under a mistaken belief, the hadd is still applied unless a valid excuse (like calling out) exists. Financial matters also follow reality: if one admits a debt that doesn't exist, it's not binding. The pattern shows that fiqh distinguishes between contexts where the mukallaf's diligence is trusted (ibadat) and where objective facts override (mu'amalat, hudud). The text concludes with a rule about divorce and emancipation: addressing one's wife mistakenly as a stranger results in divorce, and similar reasoning applies to freeing a slave.

Key Terms
الاعتبار (al-i'tibar): The legal consideration or weight given to a particular state (e.g., belief vs. reality).
ظن (zann): A strong assumption or belief held by the mukallaf, not certain knowledge.
في نفس الأمر (fi nafs al-amr): Objective reality; what is actually the case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man eats during Ramadan thinking it is still night, but it is actually after dawn, what is the ruling?
A) He must make up the fast and give kaffarah.
B) He must make up the fast without kaffarah.
C) The fast is valid and no compensation needed.
D) He must give kaffarah but does not need to make up the fast.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mukallaf's belief is considered to mitigate penalties in ibadat, but in contracts and hudud, objective reality determines the ruling, with limited exceptions.

Applications of the Rule of No Liability for Guiding and Its Exceptions
Furū' applications of universal rules on liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general rule that guiding alone creates no liability, apply the exceptions where liability arises due to breach of trust or removal of security, and distinguish between the positions of Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn on liability for indirect causation.

Core Idea
Guiding incurs liability only when a duty is breached or security is removed.

In this passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, we see practical applications of the universal rule that guiding (dilalah) does not create liability. The Sheikh presents specific cases where liability is imposed due to a failure in custody or disturbance of a protected status. We will parse each case, understand the rationale (talil), and explore an intra-Hanafi difference on liability for indirect acts such as digging a well or tearing a waterskin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Read the opening lines: the rule is that guiding to a war fort, declaring a woman free, or giving a knife to a child does not create liability in the default case.
These are instances where the guide is not in a position of trust and the harm is directly caused by another.
2
Analyze each liable case
Examine cases 1–4 and the benefit: a depositary who guides a thief (breach of trust), a guardian or agent who claims a woman is free (deception), and a muhrim who guides game outside the Haram (removal of ihram's security). Identify the common factor: removal of a protective duty or status.
The burden of proof shifts to the guide once the security is removed.
3
Contrast with non-liable cases
Consider the case of guiding to game inside the Haram: the guide is not liable because the Haram's inherent security remains intact. Similarly, the initial examples (fort, free woman statement, knife) lack any duty or security to breach.
The exception proves the rule: liability only when a breach of duty or removal of security occurs.
4
Extract the fatwa on the collector
The benefit (fā'ida) states that later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) rule that the state collector (sā'ī) is liable due to the prevalence of such appointments. Note the shift from earlier positions and the reason (ghalabat al-sa'āyah).
This shows how fatwa can evolve based on practice (urf).
5
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute
In the takmīl, compare Imam Muhammad's position (liability for digging a well, tearing a waterskin, etc.) with the Sahibayn's position (no liability, analogous to unfastening a slave's restraint). Identify the principle underlying each view.
The dispute hinges on whether the act is considered a direct or indirect cause of harm, and the presence of a duty of care.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا سهم لمن دلَّ على حصن دار الحرب ولا ضمان على من قال تزوجها فإنها حرَّة، فظهر بعد الولادة أنها أمة ولا ضمان على من دفع إلى صبي سكينا أو سلاحًا ليمسكه فقتل به نفسه وخرجت عنها مسائل : الولد .
منها : لو دل المودع السارق على الوديعة فإنه يضمن لترك الحفظ.
الثانية : لو قال ولي المرأة تزوّجها فإنها حرَّة.
الثالثة : قال وكيلها ذلك فولدت ثم ظهر أنها أمة الغير، رجع المغرور بقيمة الولد .
الرابعة : دل محرم حلالاً على صيد فقتله وجب الجزاء على الدال بشرطه في محله لإزالة الأمن بخلاف الدلالة على صيد الحرم، فإنها لا توجب شيئًا لبقاء أمنه بالمكان بعدها .
فائدة .
الخامسة : الإفتاء بتضمين الساعي، وهو قول المتأخرين لغلبة السعاية .
السادسة : لو دفع إلى صبي سكينًا ليمسكه له فوقعت عليه فجرحته كان على الدافع .
في حفر البئر ؛ قال الولي : سقط . وقال الحافر : أسقط نفسه. فالقول للحافر، كذا في التوضيح.
تکميل : يُضاف الحكم إلى حفر البئر وشق الزق وقطع حبل القنديل وفتح باب القفص على قول محمد وعندهما لا ضمان كحل قيد العبد وتمامه في شرحنا على المنار، والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
And no share for one who guides to a fort of the land of war, and no liability on one who said 'Marry her for she is free' and it appears after birth that she was a slave, and no liability on one who gave a knife or weapon to a boy to hold and he killed himself with it. And there emerged from it issues: […] the first: if the depositary guides the thief to the deposit, he is liable for abandoning custody. The second: if the woman's guardian said 'Marry her for she is free'. The third: if her agent said that and she gave birth then it appeared she was another's slave, the deceived one recovers the value of the child. The fourth: if a pilgrim in ihram guides a non-muhrim to game and he kills it, the guide must pay the penalty (jaza) due to removal of security, unlike guiding to game in the Haram which does not obligate anything because its security remains by the place after that. Benefit: The fifth: giving fatwa of liability of the collector (sa'i), which is the view of later scholars due to prevalence of collection. The sixth: if one gave a knife to a boy to hold for him and it fell on him and wounded him, liability is on the giver. Regarding digging a well: the guardian said he fell, the digger said he threw himself; the word is the digger's, as in al-Tawdih. Completion: The ruling is added to digging a well, tearing a waterskin, cutting the rope of a lantern, opening the door of a cage according to Muhammad; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) there is no liability like unfastening the restraint of a slave, and its completion is in our commentary on al-Manar, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage concludes the first section on universal rules by presenting specific applications derived from the principle that in certain contexts there is no liability (e.g., guiding to a fort, declaring a woman free, giving a weapon to a child). However, exceptions arise when there is negligence or violation of trust. The first issue: a depositary who guides a thief to the deposit is liable because he failed in his duty of care. The second: the guardian's statement that a woman is free creates liability if she turns out to be a slave. The third: the agent's similar statement leads to the deceived husband recovering the child's value. The fourth: a muhrim guiding a non-muhrim to game in the non-Haram area incurs the penalty (jaza) because he disturbed the sanctuary of ihram; but guiding in the Haram does not, as the place itself provides security. The benefit (fa'ida) notes that later Hanafi scholars rule that a state collector (sa'i) is liable due to the prevalence of such appointments. The sixth case: giving a knife to a child results in liability if injury occurs. In the well-digging dispute, the digger's word is taken because the default is that the fall was accidental. The completion (takmil) adds that according to Imam Muhammad, liability extends to similar actions like digging a well, tearing a waterskin, etc., while according to Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the two), these are not liable, analogous to unfastening a slave's restraint.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or responsibility for loss or injury, often requiring compensation.
غرور (ghurur): Deception that leads someone to act to his detriment; the deceived party is called maghrur.
دلالة (dilalah): Guiding or directing someone to a place or object; in this context, it can trigger liability if there is a breach of duty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The depositary is not liable because the thief is the direct cause.
B) The depositary is liable due to abandoning the duty of custody.
C) The depositary is liable only if the item is destroyed.
D) The depositary is not liable if he was under duress.

Key Takeaway
The rule of no liability for guiding is subject to exceptions when the guide breaches a duty of care or removes a legally protected security.

Purifiers in Hanafi Fiqh: Means of Taharah and the Nature of Purity
Rulings on purifiers from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the Hanafi purifiers and explain why division of mithli is not true purification but a concession.

Core Idea
Division of impure fungibles permits use but does not purify; true purifiers include fire, tanning, and specific physical actions.

This lesson distills the Hanafi rules on what constitutes a purifier (mutahhir) of impurity. The text lists several means, from rubbing to fire, and clarifies a disputed point: whether dividing an impure mithli (like wheat) purifies it. The Hanafi view is that it does not—it only permits use due to doubt. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correct fatwa on purity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed purifiers
Scan the passage for all items that are explicitly called purifiers (e.g., fire, tanning, draining a well).
Some actions like 'turning of the eye' refer to evaporation.
2
Analyze the dispute on division of mithli
Note that some scholars consider dividing an impure fungible (e.g., wheat) as a purifier. The author clarifies the Hanafi position: it is not actual purification but a permissibility based on doubt.
This is a key nuance in Hanafi fatwa.
3
Extract the rule on rubbing maniy
The garment is purified by rubbing maniy, except in two cases: new garment or after unwashed urine. Recognize the exceptions.
This shows that physical removal can suffice even without water.
4
Understand the broader classification
Relate these purifiers to the general Hanafi principle that purification is achieved by removal (izalah) or transformation (istihalah).
Fire transforms, tanning alters substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثوب ومسح المحاجم بالخرق المبتلة بالماء، والنار، وانقلاب العين، والدباغة، والتقوّر في الفأرة إذا ماتت في السمن الجامد، والذكاة إذا كانت من الأهل في المحل، ونزح البئر، ودخول الماء من جانب وخروجه من جانب آخر، وحفر الأرض بقلب الأعلى أسفل. وذكر بعضهم أن قسمة المثلي من المطهرات؛ فلو تنجس بر فقسم طهر. وفي التحقيق لا يطهر وإنما جاز لكل الانتفاع للشك فيها حتى لو عادت . الثوب يطهر بالفرك من المني إلا في مسألتين : قيل أن يكون الثوب جديدا، أو أمنى عقب بول لم يزله بالماء. وقد ذكرناه في شرح الكنز .
جمع
والأبوال كلها نجسة إلا بول الخفاش فإنه طاهر واختلف التصحيح في بول الهرة والفأرة (١) . ومرارة كل شيء كبوله وجرة البعير كسرقينه
الدماء كلها نجسة إلا دم الشهيد والدم الباقي في اللحم المهزول إذا قطع، والباقي في العروق، والباقي في الكبد والطحال، ودم قلب الشاة، وما لم يسل من بدن الإنسان على المختار ودم البق ودم البراغيث ودم القمل ودم السمك . فالمستثنى عشرة .
الخرء نجس إلا خرء طير مأكول، وغير مأكول على أحد القولين. وخرء الفأرة على إحدى الروايتين.
الجزء المنفصل من الحي كميتته كالأذن المقطوعة، ولاسن الساقطة إلا في حق صاحبه فطاهر. وإن كثر ما لا ينعصر إذا تنجس فلا بدَّ من التجفيف إلا في البدن فتوالي الغسلات تقوم مقامه تشترط في الاستنحاء إزالة الرائحة عن موضع الاستنجاء والأصبع التي استنجى بها إلا إذا عجز، والناس عنه غافلون. توضأ من ماء نجس وهناك من يعلمه يفترض عليه الإعلام رأى في ثوب غيره نجاسة مانعة إن غلب على ظنه أنه لو أخبره أزالها وجب وإلا فلا المرقة إذا أنتنت لا تتنجس، والطعام إذا تغير واشتدَّ تغيّره تنجس وحرم واللبن والزيت والسمن إذا أنتن لا يحرم أكله الدجاجة إذا ذبحت ونتف ريشها وأغليت في الماء قبل شقّ بطنها صار الماء نجسا وصارت نجسة بحيث لا طريق لأكلها إلا أن تحمل الهرة إليها فتأكلها .
The clothes, and rubbing of cupping tools with wet rags with water, and fire, and the turning of the eye, and tanning, and the cave-in in the mouse when it dies in solid ghee, and slaughter when from the household in the place, and draining the well, and water entering from one side and exiting from another, and digging the earth by turning the top to bottom. Some said that division of mithli (fungible) is among purifiers; so if wheat becomes impure and then divided, it becomes pure. But in reality it does not become pure, rather it is permissible for each to benefit due to doubt in it, even if it returns. The garment is purified by rubbing from maniy except in two matters: it is said that the garment should be new, or he ejaculated after urine that he did not remove with water. And we mentioned it in Sharh al-Kanz.
Collection
And all urines are impure except the urine of the bat, for it is pure, and the correct opinion varies regarding the urine of the cat and the mouse (1). And the gallbladder of everything is like its urine, and the camel's regurgitated cud is like its dung.
All bloods are impure except the blood of the martyr, and the blood remaining in lean meat when cut, and the remaining in veins, and the remaining in liver and spleen, and the blood of a sheep's heart, and what has not flowed from a human body according to the chosen opinion, and mosquito blood, and flea blood, and louse blood, and fish blood. So the exceptions are ten.
Stool is impure except the stool of a bird that is eaten, and that which is not eaten according to one of the two sayings. And the stool of the mouse according to one of the two narrations.
The separated part from a living animal is like its carrion, such as a cut ear, and the fallen tooth, except in the right of its owner, so it is pure. And if a mass that does not express liquid becomes impure, drying is necessary, except for the body, so successive washes substitute for it. In istinja, removal of smell from the place of istinja and the finger used for istinja is required, unless unable, and people are heedless of it. He performed wudu with impure water and there is someone who knows, it is obligatory for him to inform. He saw an impure obstruction on another's garment, if he is certain that if he informed him he would remove it, it is obligatory, otherwise not. If soup stinks, it does not become impure. If food changes and its change is severe, it becomes impure and forbidden. Milk, oil, and ghee, if they stink, their consumption is not forbidden. If a chicken is slaughtered, its feathers plucked, and boiled in water before opening its belly, the water becomes impure and it becomes impure such that there is no way to eat it unless a cat carries it to her and eats it.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on impurities (najasat) and purifiers from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, typical of the Hanafi school. It begins by listing various purifiers: rubbing clothes with wet clothes, fire (for impurity removal), turning of the eye (i.e., removal of impurity by evaporation), tanning hides, the collapse of a mouse in solid ghee, slaughter by a householder in the correct place, draining a well, water flow through earth, and digging earth by turning layers. The division of fungible goods (mithli) is mentioned as a purifier according to some, but the author clarifies that it does not technically purify but allows use due to doubt. The garment is purified from maniy by rubbing, except when the garment is new or when maniy occurs after urine not washed with water. Then follows a 'Collection' of impurity types: all urines are impure except bat urine; cat and mouse urine have different opinions. Gallbladder is like urine, camel's regurgitated cud is like dung. All bloods are impure except ten exceptions: martyr's blood, blood in lean meat after cutting, blood in veins, liver, spleen, sheep heart blood, blood that has not flowed from the body (the preferred view), and blood of mosquitoes, fleas, lice, and fish. Stool is impure except bird stool (both edible and non-edible birds according to one of two opinions) and mouse stool according to one narration. A part separated from a living animal is like its carrion (impure) except for the owner, for whom it is pure. For solid items that do not exude, drying suffices for purification, but for the body, successive washes replace drying. Conditions for istinja: removing smell from the place and the finger, unless inability. If one performs wudu with impure water knowingly, it is obligatory to inform. If one sees impurity on another's garment and is sure that informing will lead to removal, it is obligatory. Soup that stinks remains pure, but food with severe change becomes impure and forbidden. Milk, oil, and ghee that stink remain permissible. A chicken slaughtered, plucked, and boiled before gutting makes the water and chicken impure, and it can only be eaten if a cat eats it. These rulings are drawn from Hanafi sources like Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentaries.

Key Terms
المثلي (al-mithlī): Fungible goods that are measured or weighed and can be replaced by similar items, like wheat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered a true purifier?
A) Division of mithli
B) Fire
C) Rubbing a garment stained with maniy if the garment is new
D) Using wet rags on cupping tools

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway: true purification removes impurity physically or through transformation; division of a fungible only creates doubt that permits use but does not purify.

Categories of Impurity: Urine, Blood, Stool, and Detached Parts
Compilation of impurity categories with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the exceptions to impurity for urine, blood, stool, and detached animal parts, and apply these exceptions in fatwa.

Core Idea
All urine, blood, and stool are impure with specific exceptions for purity of bat urine, martyr's blood, and edible bird stool.

The passage provides a detailed list of liquids and solids that are considered najis in Hanafi fiqh, along with important exceptions. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correct fatwa on matters of purity, especially in cases of unavoidable contact. This lesson breaks down each category and its exceptional cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for each category
Notice the pattern: 'All urines are impure except...', 'All bloods are impure except...', 'Stool is impure except...'.
This is a common Hanafi structure: declare the general principle, then list exceptions.
2
Memorize the exceptions for urine
The only urine explicitly exempted is that of the bat. Note the ikhtilaf on cat and mouse urine.
The footnote indicates varying opinions on ta'sis (correction).
3
Count the ten exceptions for blood
List all ten: martyr's blood, blood in lean meat after cutting, in veins, liver, spleen, sheep heart blood, non-flowing blood from human (the chosen view), and blood of mosquito, flea, louse, and fish.
The phrase 'falmustathna 'asharah' helps confirm the count.
4
Analyze the rule for stool and detached parts
Stool: all impure except edible bird stool (and according to one narration, mouse stool). Detached parts: like carrion impure except for the owner (the part is pure for him).
This shows the principle of 'al-juz' al-munfasil ka al-maytah' with an ownership exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثوب ومسح المحاجم بالخرق المبتلة بالماء، والنار، وانقلاب العين، والدباغة، والتقوّر في الفأرة إذا ماتت في السمن الجامد، والذكاة إذا كانت من الأهل في المحل، ونزح البئر، ودخول الماء من جانب وخروجه من جانب آخر، وحفر الأرض بقلب الأعلى أسفل. وذكر بعضهم أن قسمة المثلي من المطهرات؛ فلو تنجس بر فقسم طهر. وفي التحقيق لا يطهر وإنما جاز لكل الانتفاع للشك فيها حتى لو عادت . الثوب يطهر بالفرك من المني إلا في مسألتين : قيل أن يكون الثوب جديدا، أو أمنى عقب بول لم يزله بالماء. وقد ذكرناه في شرح الكنز .

الخرء (al-khar'): Stool or dung of animals, considered impure unless from edible birds or according to alternative narrations.
الخفاش (al-khuffāsh): Bat; its urine is considered pure in Hanafi fiqh.

beginner

Cutting a Prayer: When Qada is Required
Ruling on qada after cutting a prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for a cut prayer and distinguish between fard, sunna, and nafl prayers.

Core Idea
Cutting a prayer requires qada except for fard and sunna, which are simply repeated.

This lesson covers a common question: if someone starts a prayer but cuts it short, must they make it up? The Hanafi school gives a clear answer: for obligatory (fard) and sunna prayers, no make-up is needed—they just perform them again. For all other prayers (nafl), make-up (qada) is required. This rule is foundational for understanding the consequences of interrupting prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prayer type
Determine if the cut prayer was fard, sunna, or nafl. This is the first analytical step because the ruling hinges on this classification.
Fard and sunna prayers are the prescribed daily prayers; nafl includes voluntary prayers.
2
Determine if the prayer was cut before completion
The ruling applies only when the prayer is intentionally cut before finishing. If completed, no issue arises.
3
Apply the rule
If the prayer is fard or sunna: no qada needed, just repeat the prayer (ada). If nafl: qada is required.
The text also covers the case of mistakenly thinking a fard was due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة
إذا شرع في صلاة وقطعها قبل إكمالها فإنه يقضيها إلا الفرض والسنن فلا قضاء فيهما، إنما يؤديهما، وكذا إذا شرع ظانًا أن عليه فرضا ولم يكن عليه. مطلقا وبالمماثل
صحيح
اقتداء الإنسان بأدنى حالاً منه فاسد مطلقا وبالأعلى صحيح إلا ثلاثة : المستحاضة والضالة والخنثى.
•
القراءة في الفرض الرباعي فرض في ركعتين إلا فيما إذا أحدث الإمام بعد الأوليين ولم يكن قرأ فيهما فاستخلف مسبوقا بهما فإنها فرض عليه في الأربع. المسبوق منفرد فيما يقضي إلا في أربع لا يقتدي ولا يُقتدى به، ولو كبر ناويًا الاستئناف صح ويتابع إمامه في سجود السهو، فإن لم يعد إليه سجد آخرها ويأتي بتكبيرات التشريق إجماعًا المسبوق لا يكون إمامًا إلا إذا استخلفه الإمام المحدث كما ذكره ملاخسرو، والمسبوق يقضي أول صلاته في حق القراءة وآخرها في حق التشهد وتمامه في البزازية لا اعتبار بنية الكافر إلا إذا قصد السفر ثلاثا ثم أسلم في أثناء المدة فإنه يقصر بناء على قصده السابق بخلاف الصبي إذا بلغ، كما في الخلاصة إذا كر آية السجدة في مكان متحد كفته واحدة إلا في مسألة؛ إذا قرأها خارج الصلاة وسجد لها ثم أعادها في مكانه في الصلاة فإنه تلزمه أخرى.
•
لا يكبر جهرًا في مسائل في عيد الأضحى، وفي يوم عرفة للتشريق، وبإزاء عدو، وبإزاء قطاع الطريق، وعند وقوع حريق، وعند المخاوف. كذا في غاية البيان. النيَّة بالقلب ولا يقوم اللسان مقامه إلا عند التعذر كما في الشرح. الدعوة المستجابة يوم الجمعة في وقت العصر عندنا على قول عامة مشايخنا، كذا في اليتيمة. إذا صحت صلاة الإمام صحت صلاة المأموم إلا إذا أحدث الإمام عامدًا بعد القعود الأخير وخلفه مسبوق فإن صلاة الإمام صحيحة دون صلاة هذا المأموم. إذا فسدت صلاة المأموم لا تفسد صلاة الإمام إلا في مسألة. اقتدى قارىء بأمي فصلاتهما فاسدة، والمسألتان في الإيضاح. إذا أدرك الإمام راكعا فشروعه لتحصيل الركعة في الصف الأخير أفضل من وصل الصف الأول متنفلاً فواتها. شرع مع بثلاث وسلَّم لزمه قضاء ركعتين شرع في الفجر ناسيًا سُنته مضى ولا يقضيها. الاشتغال بالسُّنَّة عقب الفرض أفضل من الدعاء. قراءة الفاتحة أفضل من الدعاء
Book of Prayer
If one starts a prayer and cuts it before completing it, he must make it up except for obligatory prayers and sunnas, which have no make-up, but are merely performed (again). Similarly if he starts thinking that an obligatory prayer is due upon him and it is not. Absolutely and by likeness
Correct
A person's following (iqtida) of someone lower in status than him is absolutely invalid, and following someone higher is valid except in three: the mustahada (woman with chronic bleeding), the lost (animal?), and the khuntha (hermaphrodite).
•
Reading in the four-rakat obligatory prayer is obligatory in two rakat except when the imam becomes impure after the first two and had not read in them, so he appoints a masbuq (latecomer) who has (read) those two, then reading becomes obligatory on him in all four. The masbuq is alone in what he makes up except in four matters: he does not lead nor is followed, and if he says takbir intending to begin anew, it is valid, and he follows his imam in the prostration of forgetfulness; if he does not return to him, he prostrates at the end of his prayer and performs takbirs of Tashriq by consensus. The masbuq cannot be imam unless appointed by the imam who becomes impure, as mentioned by Molla Khusrow. The masbuq makes up the first of his prayer regarding reading and the last regarding the tashahhud, and its complete mention is in al-Bazzaziyya. The intention of an unbeliever is not considered unless he intended to travel for three days then became Muslim during the period, for he shortens (prayer) based on his prior intention, unlike a minor when he reaches puberty, as in al-Khulasa. If one repeats a verse of sajdah in one place, it suffices once except in one matter: if he recites it outside prayer and prostrates for it, then repeats it in its place in prayer, another prostration is required.
•
One does not recite takbir aloud in matters: on Eid al-Adha, on the day of Arafah for Tashriq, in front of an enemy, in front of highway robbers, when a fire occurs, and during frights. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. Intention is by the heart, and the tongue does not substitute except when unable, as in al-Sharh. The answered supplication on Friday is at the time of Asr according to us, on the saying of most of our scholars, as in al-Yatima. If the imam's prayer is valid, the follower's prayer is valid except if the imam intentionally invalidates after the last sitting and a masbuq is behind him: the imam's prayer is valid but not that of this follower. If the prayer of the follower becomes invalid, the imam's prayer does not become invalid except in one matter: a literate person follows an illiterate imam, both prayers are invalid; both matters are in al-Idah. If one catches the imam in ruku, starting to attain the rakah in the last row is better than joining the first row as a supererogatory (nafl) prayer missing it. One who starts with three (rakat?) and says salam must make up two rakat. One who starts Fajr forgetting its sunna, he continues and does not make it up. Occupying oneself with sunna after the obligatory prayer is better than supplication. Reciting al-Fatihah is better than supplication.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a collection of various rulings on prayer (salah) from the Hanafi perspective, likely from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir. It begins with the rule: if one starts a prayer and cuts it before completion, he must make it up (qada), but for obligatory prayers (fard) and sunna prayers, no make-up is needed; they are simply performed again. Similarly, if one starts a prayer thinking an obligatory prayer is due but it is not, the ruling is the same. Then it states that following an imam of lower status (e.g., a sinner following a righteous person) is absolutely invalid, while following one of higher status is valid, except for three categories: a woman with istihadah (chronic uterine bleeding), a lost animal? (actually 'ḍālla' here likely refers to a stray animal, meaning its owner cannot be imam? but context unclear), and a hermaphrodite. Next is a detailed rule about reading in a four-rakat obligatory prayer: normally, reading (Quran) is obligatory in only the first two rakat. However, if the imam becomes impure (hadath) after the first two rakat and had not read in them, and appoints a latecomer (masbuq) who has read those two rakat, then reading becomes obligatory on that imam in all four rakat. The masbuq is considered alone in what he makes up, except in four matters: he does not lead nor is followed; if he intends to start anew, it is valid; he follows the imam in prostration of forgetfulness; and he performs Tashriq takbirs. The masbuq can only become imam if appointed by the original imam. The masbuq makes up the first part of his prayer regarding reading and the last part regarding tashahhud. The intention of an unbeliever is not considered, except if he intended a three-day journey and then became Muslim during the journey, he may shorten prayer based on his prior intention. Repeating a verse of sajdah in one location requires only one prostration, unless recited outside prayer then again inside. One does not recite takbir aloud on Eid al-Adha, Arafah day for Tashriq, before enemies, highway robbers, fire, or during frights. Intention is in the heart; the tongue does not substitute except when unable (like mute). The answered supplication on Friday is at Asr time according to the majority of Hanafi scholars. If the imam's prayer is valid, the follower's prayer is valid, except when the imam deliberately invalidates after the last sitting and a masbuq is behind him — then the imam's prayer is valid but the follower's is not. If the follower's prayer becomes invalid, the imam's does not, except when a literate person follows an illiterate imam — both prayers are invalid. If one catches the imam in ruku, it is better to start in the last row to attain the rakah than to join the first row as a nafl prayer missing it. If someone starts with three rakat (e.g., traveler?) and says salam, he must make up two rakat. If one starts Fajr forgetting its sunna, he continues and does not make it up. Occupying oneself with sunna after fard is better than supplication. Reciting al-Fatihah is better than supplication. These are established Hanafi rulings from authoritative texts.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qaḍā'): Making up a missed or cut prayer at a later time.
الأداء (al-adā'): Performing a prayer in its proper time; here, repeating the same prayer without qada.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of prayer does NOT require qada if cut before completion?
A) Nafl
B) Fard and sunna
C) Witr
D) All prayers

Key Takeaway
Cutting a prayer requires qada only for nafl prayers; fard and sunna prayers are simply repeated.

Iqtida (Following) : Valid and Invalid Imams
Ruling on following in prayer (iqtida)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid and invalid imams for iqtida according to Hanafi rulings, and apply the exceptions for the mustahada, al-daalla, and al-khuntha.

Core Idea
Following an imam of lower status is invalid; following a higher status is valid except for three specific cases.

This lesson focuses on who can be followed in prayer (iqtida). The general rule is that a person should not follow an imam who is lower in status (e.g., sinful vs. righteous). Following a higher-status imam is valid, but there are three exceptions: a woman with istihadah (chronic bleeding), a stray animal (meaning a person whose ownership? Actually, the term 'al-ḍālla' might refer to a lost animal or a person who is lost from the truth? In Hanafi texts, it often refers to a person who is misguided or lost, but here it is listed as an exception; we'll take it as a technical term), and a hermaphrodite (khuntha).

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle
The passage states that iqtida of a person lower in status is absolutely invalid, while iqtida of a higher status is valid.
Status here refers to religious status (righteousness vs. sinfulness).
2
Memorize the three exceptions
Even when following a higher-status imam, it is invalid in three cases: mustahada, daalla, and khuntha. Identify each.
These exceptions are specific and limited.
3
Apply the rule to scenarios
Given a scenario, decide if the iqtida is valid or invalid by checking the imam's status and whether he falls into the exceptions.
The ruling is absolute: it does not depend on other factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة
إذا شرع في صلاة وقطعها قبل إكمالها فإنه يقضيها إلا الفرض والسنن فلا قضاء فيهما، إنما يؤديهما، وكذا إذا شرع ظانًا أن عليه فرضا ولم يكن عليه. مطلقا وبالمماثل
صحيح
اقتداء الإنسان بأدنى حالاً منه فاسد مطلقا وبالأعلى صحيح إلا ثلاثة : المستحاضة والضالة والخنثى.
•
القراءة في الفرض الرباعي فرض في ركعتين إلا فيما إذا أحدث الإمام بعد الأوليين ولم يكن قرأ فيهما فاستخلف مسبوقا بهما فإنها فرض عليه في الأربع. المسبوق منفرد فيما يقضي إلا في أربع لا يقتدي ولا يُقتدى به، ولو كبر ناويًا الاستئناف صح ويتابع إمامه في سجود السهو، فإن لم يعد إليه سجد آخرها ويأتي بتكبيرات التشريق إجماعًا المسبوق لا يكون إمامًا إلا إذا استخلفه الإمام المحدث كما ذكره ملاخسرو، والمسبوق يقضي أول صلاته في حق القراءة وآخرها في حق التشهد وتمامه في البزازية لا اعتبار بنية الكافر إلا إذا قصد السفر ثلاثا ثم أسلم في أثناء المدة فإنه يقصر بناء على قصده السابق بخلاف الصبي إذا بلغ، كما في الخلاصة إذا كر آية السجدة في مكان متحد كفته واحدة إلا في مسألة؛ إذا قرأها خارج الصلاة وسجد لها ثم أعادها في مكانه في الصلاة فإنه تلزمه أخرى.
•
لا يكبر جهرًا في مسائل في عيد الأضحى، وفي يوم عرفة للتشريق، وبإزاء عدو، وبإزاء قطاع الطريق، وعند وقوع حريق، وعند المخاوف. كذا في غاية البيان. النيَّة بالقلب ولا يقوم اللسان مقامه إلا عند التعذر كما في الشرح. الدعوة المستجابة يوم الجمعة في وقت العصر عندنا على قول عامة مشايخنا، كذا في اليتيمة. إذا صحت صلاة الإمام صحت صلاة المأموم إلا إذا أحدث الإمام عامدًا بعد القعود الأخير وخلفه مسبوق فإن صلاة الإمام صحيحة دون صلاة هذا المأموم. إذا فسدت صلاة المأموم لا تفسد صلاة الإمام إلا في مسألة. اقتدى قارىء بأمي فصلاتهما فاسدة، والمسألتان في الإيضاح. إذا أدرك الإمام راكعا فشروعه لتحصيل الركعة في الصف الأخير أفضل من وصل الصف الأول متنفلاً فواتها. شرع مع بثلاث وسلَّم لزمه قضاء ركعتين شرع في الفجر ناسيًا سُنته مضى ولا يقضيها. الاشتغال بالسُّنَّة عقب الفرض أفضل من الدعاء. قراءة الفاتحة أفضل من الدعاء
Book of Prayer
If one starts a prayer and cuts it before completing it, he must make it up except for obligatory prayers and sunnas, which have no make-up, but are merely performed (again). Similarly if he starts thinking that an obligatory prayer is due upon him and it is not. Absolutely and by likeness
Correct
A person's following (iqtida) of someone lower in status than him is absolutely invalid, and following someone higher is valid except in three: the mustahada (woman with chronic bleeding), the lost (animal?), and the khuntha (hermaphrodite).
•
Reading in the four-rakat obligatory prayer is obligatory in two rakat except when the imam becomes impure after the first two and had not read in them, so he appoints a masbuq (latecomer) who has (read) those two, then reading becomes obligatory on him in all four. The masbuq is alone in what he makes up except in four matters: he does not lead nor is followed, and if he says takbir intending to begin anew, it is valid, and he follows his imam in the prostration of forgetfulness; if he does not return to him, he prostrates at the end of his prayer and performs takbirs of Tashriq by consensus. The masbuq cannot be imam unless appointed by the imam who becomes impure, as mentioned by Molla Khusrow. The masbuq makes up the first of his prayer regarding reading and the last regarding the tashahhud, and its complete mention is in al-Bazzaziyya. The intention of an unbeliever is not considered unless he intended to travel for three days then became Muslim during the period, for he shortens (prayer) based on his prior intention, unlike a minor when he reaches puberty, as in al-Khulasa. If one repeats a verse of sajdah in one place, it suffices once except in one matter: if he recites it outside prayer and prostrates for it, then repeats it in its place in prayer, another prostration is required.
•
One does not recite takbir aloud in matters: on Eid al-Adha, on the day of Arafah for Tashriq, in front of an enemy, in front of highway robbers, when a fire occurs, and during frights. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. Intention is by the heart, and the tongue does not substitute except when unable, as in al-Sharh. The answered supplication on Friday is at the time of Asr according to us, on the saying of most of our scholars, as in al-Yatima. If the imam's prayer is valid, the follower's prayer is valid except if the imam intentionally invalidates after the last sitting and a masbuq is behind him: the imam's prayer is valid but not that of this follower. If the prayer of the follower becomes invalid, the imam's prayer does not become invalid except in one matter: a literate person follows an illiterate imam, both prayers are invalid; both matters are in al-Idah. If one catches the imam in ruku, starting to attain the rakah in the last row is better than joining the first row as a supererogatory (nafl) prayer missing it. One who starts with three (rakat?) and says salam must make up two rakat. One who starts Fajr forgetting its sunna, he continues and does not make it up. Occupying oneself with sunna after the obligatory prayer is better than supplication. Reciting al-Fatihah is better than supplication.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a collection of various rulings on prayer (salah) from the Hanafi perspective, likely from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir. It begins with the rule: if one starts a prayer and cuts it before completion, he must make it up (qada), but for obligatory prayers (fard) and sunna prayers, no make-up is needed; they are simply performed again. Similarly, if one starts a prayer thinking an obligatory prayer is due but it is not, the ruling is the same. Then it states that following an imam of lower status (e.g., a sinner following a righteous person) is absolutely invalid, while following one of higher status is valid, except for three categories: a woman with istihadah (chronic uterine bleeding), a lost animal? (actually 'ḍālla' here likely refers to a stray animal, meaning its owner cannot be imam? but context unclear), and a hermaphrodite. Next is a detailed rule about reading in a four-rakat obligatory prayer: normally, reading (Quran) is obligatory in only the first two rakat. However, if the imam becomes impure (hadath) after the first two rakat and had not read in them, and appoints a latecomer (masbuq) who has read those two rakat, then reading becomes obligatory on that imam in all four rakat. The masbuq is considered alone in what he makes up, except in four matters: he does not lead nor is followed; if he intends to start anew, it is valid; he follows the imam in prostration of forgetfulness; and he performs Tashriq takbirs. The masbuq can only become imam if appointed by the original imam. The masbuq makes up the first part of his prayer regarding reading and the last part regarding tashahhud. The intention of an unbeliever is not considered, except if he intended a three-day journey and then became Muslim during the journey, he may shorten prayer based on his prior intention. Repeating a verse of sajdah in one location requires only one prostration, unless recited outside prayer then again inside. One does not recite takbir aloud on Eid al-Adha, Arafah day for Tashriq, before enemies, highway robbers, fire, or during frights. Intention is in the heart; the tongue does not substitute except when unable (like mute). The answered supplication on Friday is at Asr time according to the majority of Hanafi scholars. If the imam's prayer is valid, the follower's prayer is valid, except when the imam deliberately invalidates after the last sitting and a masbuq is behind him — then the imam's prayer is valid but the follower's is not. If the follower's prayer becomes invalid, the imam's does not, except when a literate person follows an illiterate imam — both prayers are invalid. If one catches the imam in ruku, it is better to start in the last row to attain the rakah than to join the first row as a nafl prayer missing it. If someone starts with three rakat (e.g., traveler?) and says salam, he must make up two rakat. If one starts Fajr forgetting its sunna, he continues and does not make it up. Occupying oneself with sunna after fard is better than supplication. Reciting al-Fatihah is better than supplication. These are established Hanafi rulings from authoritative texts.

Key Terms
المستحاضة (al-mustaḥāḍa): A woman with chronic uterine bleeding that is not menstruation.
الضالة (al-ḍālla): A term for a lost animal or person; in this context, likely a person who is astray or a notorious sinner.
الخنثى (al-khunthā): A hermaphrodite with ambiguous gender.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an exception to the rule that following a higher-status imam is valid?
A) Mustahada
B) Daalla
C) Khuntha
D) Traveler (musafir)

Key Takeaway
Iqtida of a lower status imam is invalid; following a higher status imam is valid except for mustahada, daalla, and khuntha.

Reading in Four-Rakat Prayer and Masbuq Rules
Detailed ruling on reading in four-rakat prayer and masbuq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on when reading (Quran) is obligatory in a four-rakat fard prayer and explain the masbuq's position regarding reading, leadership, and prostration of forgetfulness.

Core Idea
Reading in a four-rakat fard is obligatory only in the first two rakat, except when a masbuq is appointed as imam after the imam's ritual impurity.

This lesson covers a detailed scenario: in a four-rakat fard prayer, normally the imam recites Quran only in the first two rakat. However, if the imam becomes impure after the first two rakat and had not recited in them, and he appoints a masbuq (latecomer) who has already recited those two rakat, then recitation becomes obligatory on the imam in all four rakat. Additionally, we learn key rules about the masbuq: he cannot lead unless appointed, he makes up his missed rakat following specific rules regarding recitation and tashahhud, and he follows the imam's sajdat al-sahw.

Guided Path
1
Understand the normal rule
In a four-rakat fard prayer, recitation (Quran) is obligatory only in the first two rakat. This is the baseline.
This is a standard Hanafi rule.
2
Identify the exceptional scenario
The exception occurs when: (1) the imam becomes impure after the first two rakat, (2) he had not recited in those two rakat, (3) he appoints a masbuq who has already recited those two rakat as a follower. Then recitation becomes obligatory on the imam in all four rakat.
The masbuq must have completed the first two rakat with the original imam.
3
Learn the masbuq's general rules
The pasage lists four matters where the masbuq is considered alone: he does not lead unless appointed, he does not follow others in those matters, his takbir for starting anew is valid, he follows the imam in sajdat al-sahw, and he says takbir al-tashriq.
Memorize these: leading, following, sajdat al-sahw, tashriq takbirs.
4
Apply the rules to a scenario
Given a scenario with a four-rakat prayer and a masbuq, determine if reading is obligatory in all four or only two, and whether the masbuq can lead.
Practice combining the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If the follower's prayer becomes invalid, the imam's does not, except when a literate person follows an illiterate imam — both prayers are invalid. If one catches the imam in ruku, it is better to start in the last row to attain the rakah than to join the first row as a nafl prayer missing it. If someone starts with three rakat (e.g., traveler?) and says salam, he must make up two rakat. If one starts Fajr forgetting its sunna, he continues and does not make it up. Occupying oneself with sunna after fard is better than supplication. Reciting al-Fatihah is better than supplication. These are established Hanafi rulings from authoritative texts.

Key Terms
المسبوق (al-masbūq): A follower who joins the prayer late and misses one or more rak'at with the imam.
المحدث (al-muḥdith): One who becomes impure (e.g., breaks wudu) during prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the exceptional case, how many rak'at does reading become obligatory on the imam?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) It depends on the number of rak'at left

Key Takeaway
Reading in a four-rakat fard is normally wajib in two rak'at, but if the imam becomes impure without reading, it becomes wajib in all four when a masbuq is appointed.

The Rule of Cutting a Prayer Midway and Its Exceptions
Rule with exceptions on making up cut prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'if one begins a prayer and cuts it' to distinguish between prayers that require qada' and those that do not, and identify the istithna' for fard and sunnah prayers.

Core Idea
Cutting any supererogatory prayer requires qada'; cutting fard or sunnah does not.

This lesson examines a concise yet powerful legal maxim from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir that governs the consequence of voluntarily cutting a prayer after commencing it. The maxim states a general obligation to make up the prayer, but immediately carves out two exceptions: obligatory (fard) prayers and sunnah prayers. As a fatwa tool, this qawida allows a mufti to quickly determine the ruling for a wide range of cases without re-deriving from primary texts. We will parse the rule, understand its exceptions, and see how it applies to concrete scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Read the first part of the source: 'إذا شرع في صلاة وقطعها قبل إكمالها فإنه يقضيها'. This establishes the default: if someone begins any prayer and then intentionally cuts it midway, he must perform qada' (make it up later).
The rule applies to any prayer start, including nafl, sunnah, fard, etc., but note the exceptions.
2
Locate the Exception (istithna')
The words 'إلا الفرض والسنن فلا قضاء فيهما' provide the exception. The exception is broad: both fard (obligatory) and sunnah (emphasized sunnah) prayers are exempted from the qada' obligation.
The term 'السنن' here likely includes both sunnah mu'akkadah and ghayr mu'akkadah, as per the general usage in the qawida.
3
Understand the Alternative: Ada'
The text continues: 'إنما يؤديهما' meaning that instead of qada', one simply performs the prayer again within its time (ada'). This is not a make-up but a repeat.
This implies that if the time for the fard has not expired, one can repeat it; if the time has expired, qada' is not required but the prayer is lost. For sunnah, one may repeat anytime.
4
Analyze the Second Exception: Mistaken Obligation
The final clause 'وكذا إذا شرع ظانًا أن عليه فرضا ولم يكن عليه' extends the exception to a scenario where one begins a prayer thinking it is fard (e.g., a missed fard) but then realizes it is not. In this case, no qada' is needed.
This reinforces the rule: the obligation to make up only attaches to voluntarily started prayers that are inherently non-obligatory; if the intention was based on a mistake, the original obligation never existed.
5
Apply to the Furuat
Using the qawida, consider the standard furuat: (a) Nafl prayer cut → qada' obligatory. (b) Fard prayer cut → no qada', but ada' possible if time remains. (c) Sunnah prayer cut → no qada', but can repeat. Confirm these with the text.
The furuat are direct applications of the general rule and its exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا شرع في صلاة وقطعها قبل إكمالها فإنه يقضيها إلا الفرض والسنن فلا قضاء فيهما، إنما يؤديهما، وكذا إذا شرع ظانًا أن عليه فرضا ولم يكن عليه.
If one begins a prayer and cuts it before its completion, then he must make it up, except for the obligatory prayer and the sunnah prayers: there is no making up for them; rather he performs them (again). Likewise if he begins thinking that an obligatory prayer is due upon him and it is not.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage enumerates a general rule with two exceptions. The rule: commencing any prayer and then intentionally cutting it obligates a make-up (qada'). The exception: if the prayer is an obligatory (fard) prayer or a sunnah prayer, then no make-up is required; instead, one simply performs it anew (ada') if one wishes. The rationale is that a fard prayer cannot be 'made up' because its time may still be present, and a sunnah prayer is not strictly incumbent. The second exception: if one mistakenly thinks a certain prayer is obligatory and begins it, then discovers it is not, no make-up is required because the obligation never existed. This ruling applies to all prayers except the fard and sunnah as specified.

Key Terms
يقضيها (yaqḍīhā): To perform the prayer again as a make-up (qada') outside its appointed time.
يؤديهما (yu'addīhimā): To perform the prayer again within its time (ada'), not as make-up.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone begins a sunnah prayer and cuts it mid-prayer, what is the ruling?
A) He must make it up (qada').
B) He may perform it again (ada') but no qada'.
C) No action needed; it is forgiven.
D) He must offer a nafl prayer to compensate.

Key Takeaway
The qawida teaches that only voluntarily begun non-fard/non-sunnah prayers (like nafl) incur qada' when cut; fard and sunnah are exempt, allowing only ada'.

The Maxim of Following an Imam: When Status Matters
A concise maxim stating the rule and three exceptions for valid following of an imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim that following an imam of lower status is invalid, and identify the three exceptional cases where following a higher-status imam is also invalid.

Core Idea
Following an imam of lower purity or knowledge is invalid; even a higher imam is invalid if they are a mustahadah, lost, or hermaphrodite.

This lesson introduces a key maxim from the Hanafi school regarding the validity of congregational prayer (iqtida'). The maxim sets a general rule: a follower cannot be superior to the imam in terms of purity or knowledge. However, there are three exceptions where even a clearly superior follower cannot follow an otherwise valid imam. Understanding this maxim is essential for determining valid imams in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the maxim
Read the maxim: 'A person's following of one who is in a lower state than him is absolutely invalid. And following of one who is higher is valid except for three.' Note the general rule and the exception clause.
The 'state' refers to purity, knowledge of qibla, or gender status.
2
Recognize the three exceptions
The three exceptions are: mustahadah (woman with chronic bleeding), dall (one lost regarding qibla), and khuntha (hermaphrodite). Memorize them.
These are the only exceptions mentioned in this maxim.
3
Understand the rationale for each exception
For each exception, explain why the imam's own prayer is deficient: mustahadah's wudu is constantly invalid, dall's qibla direction is uncertain, khuntha's gender is ambiguous affecting leadership.
Thus they cannot lead even those who are lower in status.
4
Apply the maxim to new scenarios
Given a scenario (e.g., a pure man following a mustahadah woman), determine: Is the imam lower in status? If yes, invalid. If not lower, check if the imam is one of the three exceptions; if yes, invalid regardless. Otherwise, valid.
Practice with the furuat from the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اقتداء الإنسان بأدنى حالاً منه فاسد مطلقا وبالأعلى صحيح إلا ثلاثة : المستحاضة والضالة والخنثى.
A person's following of one who is in a lower state than him is absolutely invalid. And [following] of one who is higher is valid except for three: the mustahadah (woman with chronic bleeding), the lost (one who does not know the direction of qibla), and the hermaphrodite.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This is a concise legal maxim governing the validity of congregational prayer (iqtida'). The general rule: if the follower (muqtadi) is superior in terms of purity or knowledge, his following of an inferior imam is invalid. For example, a person who is pure cannot follow one who has an excuse (like a chronic bleeding woman) because the imam's state is deficient. Conversely, it is valid for a lower-status person to follow a higher-status imam. However, there are three exceptions where even a valid (higher) imam cannot be followed: a mustahadah (woman with istihadah), a 'dall' (one who is lost regarding qibla or has a differing ijtihad), and a hermaphrodite (khuntha) whose gender is ambiguous. These three cannot lead because their own prayer is incomplete or suspect.

Key Terms
مستحاضة (mustahāḍah): A woman with chronic uterine bleeding (istihadah) that invalidates wudu.
ضالة (ḍāllah): One who is lost regarding the qibla direction and follows his own ijtihad.
خنثى (khunthā): A hermaphrodite with ambiguous gender.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim, which of the following imams is valid for a man to follow?
A) A mustahadah woman (chronic bleeding)
B) A lost person (uncertain qibla)
C) A hermaphrodite
D) A person who is higher in knowledge and purity

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim: following a lower-status imam is invalid; following a higher-status imam is valid unless the imam is a mustahadah, lost, or hermaphrodite.

Recitation Obligation in Four-Rak'ah Fard Prayer
A qa'idah with an exception regarding recitation obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule and exception for recitation in four-rak'ah fard prayer and apply it to a new congregational scenario.

Core Idea
Recitation in four-rak'ah fard is fard in two rak'ahs, except when imam nullifies and a latecomer successor must recite in all four.

This passage presents a key qa'idah (maxim) from the Hanafi school that specifies when recitation (Quran) is obligatory in the four-rak'ah fard prayer (Zuhr, Asr, Isha). It establishes a clear general rule and a narrow exception. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti to correctly advise on prayer validity in cases of imam nullification and substitution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Read the beginning of the text: 'القراءة في الفرض الرباعي فرض في ركعتين'. This states that recitation is fard in two rak'ahs. Understand that in a normal four-rak'ah fard prayer, recitation is obligatory only in the first two rak'ahs.
The general rule covers all four-rak'ah fard prayers unless a specific condition triggers the exception.
2
Identify the Exception
Read the exception: 'إلا فيما إذا أحدث الإمام بعد الأوليين ولم يكن قرأ فيهما فاستخلف مسبوقا بهما فإنها فرض عليه في الأربع'. This specifies three conditions: (1) imam nullifies after the first two rak'ahs, (2) he had not recited in those two rak'ahs, (3) he appoints a latecomer (masbuq) who followed in those two as his successor. In that case, recitation becomes fard upon the successor in all four rak'ahs.
The exception is very specific; all conditions must be met.
3
Understand the Rationale
The latecomer, as a follower, did not recite in the first two rak'ahs (since the imam didn't recite, and the follower is silent). When he becomes imam, he must compensate for the missing recitation by reciting in both the two he already prayed as follower and the two he leads. This ensures the obligation of recitation is fulfilled in the congregation's prayer.
This exception is rooted in the principle of necessity to preserve the prayer's validity.
4
Apply to a New Scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether the general rule or exception applies. Check the three conditions: (a) Did the imam nullify after exactly two rak'ahs? (b) Did he fail to recite in those two? (c) Did he appoint a latecomer who followed in those two? Only if all three are met does the exception apply.
Common mistake: applying the exception when the imam nullifies after one rak'ah or after reciting. In those cases, the general rule of recitation in two rak'ahs remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القراءة في الفرض الرباعي فرض في ركعتين إلا فيما إذا أحدث الإمام بعد الأوليين ولم يكن قرأ فيهما فاستخلف مسبوقا بهما فإنها فرض عليه في الأربع.
Recitation in the four-rak'ah fard prayer is obligatory in two rak'ahs, except when the imam nullifies his prayer after the first two rak'ahs and had not recited in them, and he appoints as successor a latecomer who followed him in those two [rak'ahs], then recitation becomes obligatory upon him in all four [rak'ahs].
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage states the general rule that in a four-rak'ah fard prayer (like Zuhr, Asr, Isha), recitation of Quran (Fatiha and a surah) is only obligatory (fard) in the first two rak'ahs. In the last two rak'ahs, one may recite quietly or remain silent. However, there is an exception: if the imam becomes invalid (e.g., his wudu breaks) after the first two rak'ahs, and he had not yet recited anything in those two rak'ahs, and he appoints as his successor a latecomer (masbuq) who joined him in those first two rak'ahs, that successor must now recite in all four rak'ahs (the two he already prayed as follower and the two he will complete as imam). This is because the latecomer, when he steps forward, starts leading the remaining two rak'ahs, but he must compensate for the missing recitation in the first two by reciting in all four. The exception is limited to that specific scenario.

Key Terms
فرض (farḍ): Obligatory; recitation is a fard (essential) act in the prayer.
أحدث (aḥdatha): To nullify wudu (i.e., break one's ablution), making the prayer invalid.
مسبوق (masbūq): A latecomer who missed one or more rak'ahs of the congregational prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a normal four-rak'ah fard prayer, in how many rak'ahs is recitation obligatory?
A) All four
B) First two
C) Last two
D) None

Key Takeaway
Recitation in four-rak'ah fard is fard in the first two rak'ahs, except in the specific scenario of imam nullifying after two without reciting and appointing a latecomer successor.
